Building the quantum-AI future with Hartmut Neven and James Manyika

Google for Developers(2.6M)·40:28·3.4K views·2026-05-21
Hartmut NevenVP of Engineering, Google James ManyikaSVP of Research, Technology, and Society, Google
TL;DRGoogle’s Quantum AI division is rapidly accelerating its timeline toward commercial viability, demonstrating breakthroughs in below-threshold error correction and quantum machine learning that lower the hardware threshold for practical applications…
and mandate a...

TL;DR

Google’s Quantum AI division is rapidly accelerating its timeline toward commercial viability, demonstrating breakthroughs in below-threshold error correction and quantum machine learning that lower the hardware threshold for practical applications and mandate a transition to post-quantum encryption by 2029.

The Foundations and Hardware of Google Quantum AI

  • Superposition as a Superpower: Quantum computing derives its processing capabilities from superposition—the ability of a quantum system to exist in multiple configurations simultaneously.
      • The Willow Chip: Google's 105-qubit Willow chip can process $2^{105}$ configurations in a single clock cycle, completing calculations in minutes that would take a classical supercomputer 10 septillion years.
  • Superconducting Architecture: Google builds macroscopic qubits using loops of superconducting wire (Josephson circuits). This allows quantum phenomena to be engineered at a macroscopic scale rather than attempting to manipulate individual microscopic atoms.
      • Ultra-Cold Engineering: To eliminate thermal noise, Google's quantum chips are cooled to 10 millikelvin (one-hundredth of a degree above absolute zero), making the environment colder than deep space.
  • Exploration of Alternative Pathways: While Google's primary path utilizes superconducting qubits, the company has established a new laboratory in Boulder, Colorado, to pursue a neutral atom approach. This parallel track may offer distinct advantages in quantum-enhanced sensing.

Key Milestones and Algorithmic Breakthroughs

  • The Error Correction Hurdle: Keeping fragile superposition states alive (coherence time) is the key challenge of quantum hardware. Google has extended coherence times from 20 microseconds to over 250 microseconds.
      • Below-Threshold Breakthrough: In 2024, the Willow chip demonstrated a twofold reduction in errors when physical qubits were scaled up to form a logical qubit. This "below-threshold" milestone proved that physical scaling successfully suppresses errors.
  • Accelerating the Commercial Roadmap: Algorithmic advancements have dramatically reduced the physical hardware requirements for a commercially viable system.
      • Milestone 5 is the New Milestone 6: Previously, Google estimated a commercial system would require 1 million physical qubits (Milestone 6). Due to algorithmic efficiency, a commercially relevant machine is now projected to require only 100,000 physical qubits (Milestone 5).
  • First Useful Quantum Algorithm: Google, in partnership with UC Berkeley, demonstrated Quantum Echoes, the first quantum machine learning algorithm applied to a real-world scientific task.
      • Chemical Discovery: The algorithm was used to analyze nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) data to solve an open chemistry question: measuring the dihedral rotation angles of diphenyl molecules.

The Bidirectional Synergy of "Quantum AI"

  • AI Accelerating Quantum: Artificial intelligence is being utilized to solve complex quantum engineering tasks.
      • AlphaQubits: A collaboration between Google DeepMind and the Quantum AI team applied machine learning to significantly optimize quantum error correction decoding.
  • Quantum Accelerating AI: Quantum computers can generate synthetic datasets to train classical AI models in domains where real-world data collection is bottlenecked.
      • Data Generation: While projects like AlphaFold required 50 years of physical data collection to train, a quantum computer can simulate molecular and material science data rapidly. Google has already begun sharing quantum-generated material science datasets with DeepMind.

Cryptanalysis and the Post-Quantum Timeline

  • The Threat to Current Encryption: Quantum computers running Shor’s algorithm can crack modern cryptographic standards, such as RSA and Elliptic Curve Cryptography (ECC), by easily solving complex mathematical factoring problems.
  • Collapsing Timelines: Refinements in quantum algorithms have drastically lowered the estimated hardware size required to break encryption standards.
      • RSA-2048: The estimated physical qubit requirement to crack RSA-2048 has dropped from 20 million to just 1 million.
      • ECC-256: Digital signatures and cryptocurrencies using ECC-256 could be compromised with only a few hundred thousand physical qubits.
  • The 2029 Target: Google has published guidelines advising organizations that they must transition to post-quantum cryptographic schemes by 2029 to protect against future quantum decryptional capabilities.

***

"Temperature is really the physicist's term for noise, and we don't want to have noise in our chips. So the colder we can make it, the better." — Hartmut Neven
"A real powerful future AI wants to have access... to the most potent, capable computational substrate we can think of, and currently that is quantum computing." — Hartmut Neven
"Because of this progress, advances in algorithms, we now think that a commercially relevant machine would only need about 100,000 physical qubits... milestone five is the new milestone six." — Hartmut Neven
Good morning, and thank you for coming to this session this morning. My name is James Manyika, and I'm the President for Research, Labs, Technology & Society at Google and Alphabet. You know, Google has an incredible track record and history of pursuing very ambitious problems, challenges, and moonshots. Some would argue that, in fact, search itself is a moonshot, or has been a moonshot. The work in AI, arguably, has been a moonshot. Some of the earliest examples of this at Google were back in 2001, when Google started to do things like auto-correction using machine learning. Then, you could also think about the work on Waymo, driverless cars, as being a moonshot that has now turned into reality as we start to ride Waymos. Hopefully, you've all done that. Quantum is one of those, too. We've focused on trying to build the idea of quantum computers for quite a long time, and that's what we're going to spend time talking about this morning. I should say that the foundations on which we're building quantum computers go all the way back to the 1980s, when researchers at Berkeley—Michel Devoret, John Martinis, and John Clarke—started to work on this idea of how to do superconductivity. Up until that time, the understanding was that quantum phenomena were only observable at the micro level, so the question was: could you ever make observations at the macro level? They made these incredible experiments and breakthroughs that led to the idea of circuits you could actually build at the macroscopic scale. That has been the foundation of how we think about building quantum computers. I should say those three individuals, Michel Devoret, John Martinis, and John Clarke, were awarded the Nobel Prize in Physics last year. I'm actually glad to say two of them, Michel Devoret and John Martinis, have actually been part of our quantum team at Google. While John Martinis is no longer with us on the quantum team, Michel Devoret is actually our chief hardware scientist on the quantum computing team at Google. I'm going to be joined in this conversation by Hartmut Neven. Hartmut is the leader and founder of our quantum AI team. Hartmut founded this team back in 2012 with the idea of how we can build and achieve this moonshot of building quantum computers. He and I are going to have a conversation. We work together, so it's always a pleasure for me to chat with Hartmut. We'll talk about first of all what quantum computing is, where we are, what progress we are making, and what are some of the challenges, opportunities, and things that we're excited about, as well as what we can look forward to. So, Hartmut, thanks for joining me. Oh, a pleasure. Thanks, everyone, for coming. Well, let's start. You know, quantum computing or quantum mechanics is one of those very complex topics. How would you describe quantum computing? Give us some basics. What are some of the basic things that one would have to understand about quantum computing? So, if you want to understand where the superpower of quantum computers comes from, the one concept we need to grasp is the concept of superposition. Let me give you a quick quantum computing 101 and explain what superposition is. Essentially, quantum physics states that any system can simultaneously exist in many configurations. That's what is called a superposition. To help you visualize it, consider the following: let's look at the system we are forming here right now in this room, and we are sitting here in one specific configuration. But you can easily envision another one. For instance, the two of you could switch seats, or James and I could switch seats. So, there are other configurations. A reading of quantum physics would suggest that all of these, or many of these constellations and configurations, are realized right now and they evolve together in time. That is what we take advantage of in our chips. But, but I think one of the things that often... so, how would you describe, because we go from trying to build physical qubits to then logical qubits? Explain the idea of qubits, and how should we think about what that is. So, a qubit is short for quantum bit, and it's essentially a bit, or qubits are a number of bits, that I can put into superposition. I will later show you—or maybe we do this right now—I will show you a chip that contains qubits. I'm not sure if the camera can see this, but this is a Willow chip, one of our more recent chips, and it has 105 qubits in there. Now that you know what a superposition is, you can envision the following: those qubits can be in a superposition state. A single configuration for 105 qubits is just a string of zeros and ones, call it a bit string of length 105. But similar to what we envisioned before, you can just flip a few qubits, and you get a different bit string. You flip a few more, and you get a different bit string. How many bit strings can there be? If there are 105 quantum bits in this chip, it is 2 to the power of 105. And then, as amazing as this may sound, in just one clock cycle of this chip, we can touch 2 to the 105 different objects, 2 to the 105 different strings. That is an enormous number. Therefore, you arrive at these situations where this tiny chip can do certain computations that the largest factory-sized data center would need an inordinate amount of time to compute—10 septillion years in a recent demonstration. In my, in my opening remarks, I mentioned the idea that we're building on breakthroughs that our colleagues, Michel Devoret and others, made in the 1980s. One of the key things that they worked on was this idea of superconductivity as a way—with the ideas of how do you do, kind of, if you like, quantum tunneling with Josephson circuits—to be able to achieve superconductivity. That often requires that we actually cool these things down to a few degrees above Kelvin. So, in the lab, describe the fact that you're actually building these extraordinary fridges so you can cool things down to a few degrees above zero Kelvin. Why are we building it that way, or how does that work? So, there are different ways you can make qubits. In quantum mechanics, famously, you might think of quantum mechanics as the physics of the very small—the thing that describes electrons and atoms. But that is actually incorrect. Quantum physics is the law of the land at all scales, from the microscopic all the way to the universe as a whole. It just happens to be the case that quantum effects can be more easily controlled, managed, and observed on microscopic scales. So, a natural choice to build a qubit, therefore, would be to just use an atom—to use, let's say, the states of a spin, of an electron, or of a nucleus. But these things are tiny and hard to engineer with. The ingenious invention that got the Nobel Prize, as you explained, was to essentially make macroscopic qubits. By using a loop of superconducting wires, you can have the same property of quantized energy levels and other hallmark features of atoms, but at a macroscopic scale. This makes it much easier to put into a chip and work with. And in fact, one of the things that you always like to say, Hartmut, is that your lab is probably the coldest place in our galaxy. Can you explain that? Yeah, to work with these artificial atoms made out of superconducting material—and many of you may know that to get superconductivity, which is a macroscopic quantum effect, you have to cool down the metals or materials to a very low degree. We currently work with aluminum qubits; we could have used niobium or tantalum, there are some choices, but all of them have to go down to about one Kelvin, which is pretty cool. But we don't stop there. We go even further down to just 10 millikelvin, because temperature is really the physicist's term for noise, and we don't want to have noise in our chips. The colder we can make it, the better. Right. So, talk a little bit about what is difficult about building quantum computers. What are some of the key challenges you have to solve if you're trying to build these systems? In the very beginning, I explained to you that the key resource is superposition states, and we need to keep these superpositions of different constellations alive. The time that measures this is an important metric; we call it the coherence time of a quantum computer. It essentially measures how long these superpositions survive, and you want to make this as long as possible. Initially, we all thought, "Oh, this is the key battle to fight, the key engineering challenge to get right." But it turned out, in this area, we made rapid progress. We went from a 20-microsecond coherence time to 10 times more—now over 250 microseconds—which is pretty much enough for what we need for useful quantum computing. So, it is not the key physics challenge anymore; it is more mundane, system-level issues that hold us back. So, why would anybody want to build a quantum computer? What are some of the useful applications, assuming we get there? We'll talk a little bit in a little while about where we are, but why would you want a quantum computer? Yeah, so on the application side, there are various areas. One thing that is important for the audience to understand is that quantum computers, as far as we know, will always be sort of a specialist tool. It will be very good for certain applications and not so useful for others. So, what are some of the useful applications we can think of? The first one is what we call Richard Feynman's killer app. Feynman was the first one to realize that if you want to simulate a system where quantum effects are important, you run against this combinatorial challenge where you have to consider all these different constellations, and that is just impossibly hard for classical computers to do. He coined, as you saw in the initial video, that if you want to make a faithful simulation of nature, you had better make it quantum mechanical. But I think often when people think about quantum computers, they think about the speed-up benefits—that it will be able to run things much faster. But also, there are some problems that are inherently quantum, that you can only work on. Can you make a distinction between where we are mostly capitalizing just on speed-ups, versus when we are actually working on things that can only be worked on with quantum computers? Let's name a few examples. I just gave you this general overview: Richard Feynman's killer app, the simulation of quantum systems. That is actually a task that is necessary more often than you might think. Let's pick a nice one: batteries. It sounds a little bit boring, but we would all feel so much better if we could fly in an airplane that is powered by batteries, not by kerosene, when we go on vacation. Currently, there are things like lithium-air batteries, which have a higher energy density than kerosene, so you could perfectly do that. Unfortunately, they are very brittle and have various material science issues, which is why we don't use them today. A quantum computer would be a marvelous tool to simulate various designs for lithium-air batteries, and then we can have them not just in cars, but in airplanes, too. Yeah, I recall a comment—maybe you can correct me—that there are currently something like 70+ kinds of algorithms and problems that people in the community have actually identified as things where a quantum computer would be the most useful way to tackle them. Is it still 70, or is that list growing? Yeah, thanks for mentioning this number. I should have checked up on the website, because when we started talking, it was like 60, and now it is 70. I hope that in a few years, there will be 600, 700—ten times more—important algorithms where quantum computers give you a decisive advantage. So, Hartmut, let's talk about where we are at this stage. I remember when you and I first started working together, I guess five years ago, you had a roadmap which had six milestones. Can you describe the roadmap you've had for progress towards building a quantum computer? Where are we on that roadmap, and what progress are we making? Originally—and if anybody has watched the work of the Google Quantum AI team, you may have seen we typically show this first thing whenever we give a talk or presentation—our mission is to build a useful quantum computer for otherwise unsolvable problems. To get to that, we set out a roadmap with six milestones to build such a machine. The first two milestones we cleared a few years ago. Our very first one was in 2019, where we showed for the first time that a quantum computer could compute something quickly in minutes that would have taken many, many years on a classical supercomputer to do. That was milestone one. Milestone two was showing a technique called quantum error correction, which is essentially the technology you need to keep those superposition states alive. That was well understood in theory since the '90s, but in '22, for the first time, we reduced this to practice and built a machine where quantum error correction successfully reduced the errors in a machine in practice. We are now ahead of—hopefully, fingers crossed, we solve it later this year—milestone three. This would be one very good module out of which you can build a large, modular quantum computer. By the time we get to hopefully milestone five or six, we will have useful applications. But let's talk a little bit about some of the recent breakthroughs. You showed the Willow chip, which is a chip we showed the world back in 2024, and I think there were two key breakthroughs associated with that. One was the fact that it was able to do in a few minutes what would take the world's fastest supercomputer 10 septillion years—that's a one with 24 zeros next to it, which is extraordinary. The other, in some ways more important, demonstration was this idea of below-threshold error correction. Can you explain what that is and why that matters so much? Quantum error correction is the technology you need to keep the important superposition states alive. For the very first time, as I said, we cleared that hurdle in '22. But the community wasn't too impressed because the errors just came down by a few percent, and people said, "Well..." How do you do error correction? You typically use redundancy; that's what we use in engineering to make things safer. You take a number of physical qubits, as we call them, and orchestrate them into one more stable logical qubit. That is what error correction does. Before, when people tried it, they introduced more redundancy, but there was more stuff: more qubits, more wires, more measurement devices, so the error always went up. In '22, we made it come down for the first time, but by an unimpressive few percent. With Willow, we basically did the same experiment again in '24, but now it was a much higher-quality chip, and the error came down by a factor of two. Then the field, for the first time, thought, "Oh, this seems to really work for good." So that was an important breakthrough. Last year, we had another big breakthrough. To set up that breakthrough: all these typical benchmarks we have been using, such as the 10 septillion years and so forth, were talking about using a particular benchmark mechanism called random circuit sampling to measure progress, which is not a very useful computation. But the breakthrough last year with quantum echoes was actually the first time anyone had shown a useful algorithm. Can you talk about quantum echoes and why that breakthrough was important? Yes, you said it nicely. The initial demonstrations that quantum chips can do something impressive were done on exotic mathematical benchmarks—essentially, problems that you or people in the real world don't have, which are only good for benchmarking. Then the criticism was, "Okay, show us something that I would care about." Quantum echoes was the answer to this. It was the first quantum algorithm that can do a useful task. And talk about the task, because we had a demonstration where we worked with researchers at Berkeley and others to actually show a useful use of quantum echoes. Can you describe that work? Yes, so quantum echoes is essentially the quintessential quantum machine learning algorithm. It allows you to learn from readily available data—data that comes from nuclear magnetic resonance imaging, like the NMR machines that pharmaceutical companies use, or if you go to a hospital and do an MRI scan. To get more information out of these devices, that is where the quantum echoes algorithm applies. And I think in that example, what you were able to observe was to show that, for the first time, we are able to measure these kinds of dihedral angles of these very hard-to-understand molecules. I think, was it a couple of benzene molecules? Can you... Yeah, typically when you have such a new algorithm, there are a few steps to really lodge it in practice. Initially, we showed it on some proof-of-principle problems, and then you go closer to what chemists care about. So there was a molecule called diphenyl, which has two benzene rings, and chemists didn't know how these two rings were rotated relative to each other. We computed this angle using quantum echoes and showed, "Look, you can answer real open questions in chemistry with that algorithm." You know, the quantum team at Google is actually called Quantum AI, and we talk about why that name was chosen. There is always a question about whether these two things need each other. Does quantum computing need AI, and does AI need quantum computing? Can you explain the origin of the name Quantum AI for the team? I would love to debate you a little bit on this question of AI and quantum—why that name? Why is our team called Quantum AI? I worked in AI for most of my career—actually, my PhD was on self-driving cars, and I joined Google doing computer vision and visual search. But at the time, it dawned on me that a truly powerful future AI would want to have access to the most potent, capable computational substrate we can think of, and currently that is quantum computing. I thought Quantum AI should be able to do tasks that AI running on classical machines will never be able to do. In 2012, we set out, based on this thesis, to show that this is really true. I think one of the reasons, one of the contributions that quantum computing can make to AI, is in particular datasets. Yeah, so maybe we should advertise this, James. For those of you who want to know more details, there will be a special issue of Daedalus coming out—you are the editor—and there we put an essay called "Quantum + AI = Quantum AI". It speaks about this bidirectional acceleration: how we use AI today to accelerate progress in quantum computing, and how, starting now and even more so in the future, quantum resources will help AI solve novel tasks. One of those examples, which was incredible to see, was how there was a deep collaboration between the DeepMind team and the quantum team around this idea of AlphaQubits. This was a way to think about how to use AI and machine learning techniques to actually help the progress we have been making on error correction. It was one example where AI was actually benefiting quantum. Have there been other examples like that? Let me also answer your previous question properly: how can quantum help AI? There are probably a dozen different ways, but let me give you one or two examples. Take AlphaFold, a resounding success used in labs all over the world. To train AlphaFold, we needed datasets that started to be collected in the 1970s. It took 50 years to build things like the Protein Data Bank to train AlphaFold—very painstaking work by many PhDs, supported by lots of government grants. A quantum computer would be able to produce very valuable training sets in many settings where it is difficult to make those training sets otherwise. We expect this to be a very first contribution of quantum to AI, where we say, "Look, here is a great dataset that you should use." Actually, as a late-breaking piece of news, just last week we had our first datasets relevant to material science where we thought, "Oh wow, the quality is good enough, we should ship them to our colleagues at DeepMind." That's fantastic. One of the things that I find pretty exciting is that, even though of course we are on this moonshot journey to build a fully error-corrected quantum computer, there is a lot of great science and discovery happening along the way. Can you give a couple of examples? One of my favorite ones was the holographic effect, which goes back to a conjecture that Einstein had. Talk a little bit about some of the examples of the incredible science you and the team are able to do along the way. Now that we have chips with a few hundred qubits, we can make good on Richard Feynman's dream to simulate quantum systems, study their properties, and learn new things. You mentioned a few already. One very exotic one, which created quite a firestorm in the scientific community, is hard to explain fully. You might have heard of the effect of quantum entanglement, where two quantum particles can be correlated at long distances. You may also have heard about wormholes, which are connections through space between two distant space points. There is a field in physics called holographic duality that claims these two processes are the same. If that is really true, then we can use entangled qubits, which we can produce on our chip, to make wormholes. We essentially created a wormhole—and I use the word "created", not "simulated", because, strictly speaking, if the string theorists are right and this duality exists, then a tiny wormhole was actually created. Don't worry, you wouldn't have fallen in. This gets very exotic pretty quickly. You have also been doing experiments on quantum gravity, as well as understanding deeper questions about electromagnetism that we didn't fully understand before. There is a lot of incredible science going on along the way. One thing we are proud of is that our team produces a dense string of high-impact publications discussing exactly these discoveries made through quantum simulation of interesting systems. Just to name them, without fully explaining: time crystals. We made time crystals, which is the closest physics lets you get to a perpetuum mobile. It is essentially a system that stays in periodic motion forever without needing to exchange any energy with the environment. Another exotic thing was non-abelian anyons. I know that's a mouthful, but it is something that seemingly goes against common sense. Imagine you build a little house out of Lego blocks. If there are two bricks that look the same and you switch them out, when you look again at your house, nothing should have changed because they were identical. In quantum physics, there was a prediction that this may be different—that even though you swap two identical particles, the overall system changes properties. We showed in practice that this really exists. Often when people think about quantum computing, you and I can talk about all these extraordinary applications in biology, chemistry, and materials, but the one application most people think about is encryption. Ever since Shor's algorithm, we have known that most of our cryptographic and encryption systems rely on the fact that these are incredibly hard problems for classical computers to solve. We rely on the difficulty of those computations as a way to secure our encryption systems. But if you had a quantum computer, you could probably crack those systems. Recently, you and the team made some news suggesting that the ability to build systems that can crack RSA encryption and elliptic curve cryptography systems is not that far away. Can you talk a little bit about what the news was and what the progress has been on building those kinds of systems? Fortunately, many of the natural applications for quantum computers are very friendly to humankind, like designing drugs quicker, making better batteries, fusion reactors, and so on. But one of the more problematic applications of quantum computing is that it can be very powerful for cryptanalysis. Indeed, in quantum computing, it is not just the hardware that has made progress; the algorithms have made progress as well. We were able to show that you need fewer resources to do these simulations and cryptanalysis. Therefore, the timelines are moving inward. It is not something where you can wait until the late 2030s before you have to worry about it. Actually, Google put out a blog post saying, "Hey, we need to move to more powerful post-quantum crypto schemes already by 2029." To put this in concrete terms, back in 2019, the view in the field was that if you wanted a quantum system capable of cracking RSA encryption, you would need at least a 20-million-qubit system. We showed about a year ago that the threshold had actually come down because of progress to where you would need perhaps only a million qubits. Then, two months ago, we showed that with elliptic curve cryptography systems—which are mostly used for cryptocurrencies and other things—the threshold had come down even further. This suggests that the day in which we have to move to post-quantum cryptography systems is not that far away. Talk about how you think about our responsibilities when it comes to making sure the world is ready for the arrival of quantum computer systems. You're exactly right. Last year, a very gifted quantum algorithm designer on our team named Greg Gidney showed that you can do cryptanalysis on RSA 2048 with just a million physical qubits. Then he went on to show that for ECC 256, which is important for digital signatures, you need only a few hundred thousand physical qubits. By the way, earlier when we described the roadmap, I should have added this: our roadmap has six milestones, and number six was essentially to build a physical chip or a set of physical chips with a million physical qubits. But because of this progress in algorithms, we now think that a commercially relevant machine would only need about 100,000 physical qubits. Our meme there is that milestone five is the new milestone six; that is when we would really see commercial impact. So, Hartmut, as you look ahead, what are you most excited about? And what are you most concerned about with quantum computing? What I'm most excited about is that right now, to argue that many quantum algorithms are useful in real life, you have to prove it by pencil and paper because you can't run the algorithm yet. At the end of the decade, we won't have to do this anymore. If there is a cool quantum machine learning algorithm, rather than painstakingly trying to prove it—and we can't prove these things typically for classical neural networks either—we will just demonstrate it by running it. That will be a much more fun period, when empirically and heuristically you can test out and develop new algorithms. But I do know you're excited about a few other things, too. Maybe I can draw you out on this. You've been thinking about the intersection of quantum computing and creativity, for example. Can you talk about some of the experiments you've been running? We recently did some very fun experiments. Many of you may know that a key tool to generate nice images, audio, and video is diffusion neural networks. They essentially take a noise tensor and are trained to sculpt a nice image out of this noise. We had the idea that one of the things we can do today is represent noise distributions—or probability distributions, I should say—on our chips that are outside the reach of classical computers. We asked, "If we use our chip to make a noise tensor, will this make a more vibrant, better-looking image?" We produced those images, and actually, you are going to see them. We are trying to find ways to release tools so you can play with them and judge for yourself whether this hypothesis is correct. But you actually showed some of this at some exhibitions in Europe recently, I think, right? Yeah, we had collaborations with known artists, starting with Refik Anadol in 2020 where we did the same process. We used output from a quantum processor—a quantum noise tensor—to build this artwork. It was called "Quantum Memories" and was actually very successful. It was seen by 1.3 million viewers, not just clicking on YouTube, but physical people coming into the museum. There were two other shows in Berlin, and one I was very proud of: a show that drew a crowd that formed a one- or two-mile-long line in Berlin in the middle of winter to get into the Kraftwerk exhibition to see this quantum art show. That was very nice to see. So, Hartmut, as we wrap up, we are at the end of our time. If we are having this conversation a couple of years from now, what kinds of things do you hope we will have achieved? What new results will you be able to show? I hope to show—and our team is very motivated by this—that this basic thesis is correct: that Quantum AI is a more powerful form of AI. Well, actually, maybe I can sneak in one other question. At the very beginning of this conversation, we focused on the fact that you've been pursuing superconductivity and superconducting qubits as the way to build quantum computers. But that is clearly not the only way to build quantum computers. There are other mechanisms; people talk about neutral atoms, topological qubits, and a whole range of possibilities. You recently started a new team focused on neutral atoms. Can you describe that as an alternative way to build these systems? Yes, there are five, six, or seven ways being explored to represent quantum bits. Earlier, I explained to you how superconductivity is a very good strategy to do this because you have macroscopic atoms that are easy to deal with. Now, it turns out that the people who work with neutral atoms have also made nice progress, to the point where we thought, "Maybe they might get to certain applications earlier than the superconducting path." We added a second track, and we now have an emerging lab in Boulder, Colorado, where we pursue this neutral atom approach to quantum computing. We will see how this goes, and, of course, both approaches can benefit tremendously from the common expertise we have in algorithms, error correction, control electronics, and other areas. And they both have very different properties, which makes them very complementary, I think, right? Yeah, they both have their pluses and minuses. Superconducting will always be much quicker. Neutral atom systems have maybe an easier way to be connected to sensors, which is something we haven't talked about yet. Quantum-enhanced sensing literally gives humanity new eyes to see features of the universe we so far were blind to. I think we're being told we should wrap this up, but thank you for the conversation. I hope this was useful and informative to everybody. Good to see you, Hartmut, as always. Yeah, that was a fun conversation. Thank you, James. Thank you.

Powell Is Out, Warsh Is In: What It Means for Crypto

Unchained(72.8K)·57:40·69 views·2026-05-21
Noelle AchesonAuthor, Crypto Is Macro Now Jerome PowellFormer Chair, Federal Reserve Kevin WarshFormer Federal Reserve Governor
TL;DRAs Kevin Warsh prepares to succeed Jerome Powell as Federal Reserve Chair, global bond yields signal severe macro tightening while stock markets reach all-time highs on AI hype.
Meanwhile, the crypto sector is adjusting to major structural shifts, highlighted by...

TL;DR

As Kevin Warsh prepares to succeed Jerome Powell as Federal Reserve Chair, global bond yields signal severe macro tightening while stock markets reach all-time highs on AI hype. Meanwhile, the crypto sector is adjusting to major structural shifts, highlighted by Coinbase’s strategic alignment with Hyperliquid to boost global USDC distribution and anticipation of the U.S. Clarity Act.

The Divergence: Stock Market Hype vs. Bond Market Reality

  • A Growing Valuation Gap: Global bond yields are climbing, signaling global tightening, while the stock market continues to reach new highs.
      • The Equal-Weight Discrepancy: The gap between the market-cap-weighted S&P 500 and the equal-weight index is widening at a rate not seen since 1999, right before the dot-com bust, indicating an incredibly top-heavy market.
      • The AI Momentum: The stock market remains swept up in hype cycles, particularly around AI and chip sectors like Nvidia. Acheson warns that forward P/E ratios are based on company guidance that assumes endless infrastructure demand, creating underlying market fragility.
  • Sticky Inflation Indicators: Core CPI has hovered flatly between 2.6% and 3.0% since 2024, proving that disinflation has stalled.
      • The Hormuz Crisis Legacy: Even if the Strait of Hormuz crisis resolves, it will take months for energy price declines to filter through to core inflation indices.
      • Gas Price Psychology: In the U.S., rising pump prices drive up consumer inflation expectations, which in turn influences actual inflation behavior.

The "BLISS Trade" and Fiscal Moral Hazard

  • Structural Stimulus over the "Trump Trade": Former IMF Chief Economist Gita Gopinath coined the term BLISS ("Big, Large, and Sustained Stimulus") to describe a structural shift in global governance.
      • No Pain Allowed: Regardless of political party, modern administrations will spend aggressively to bail out citizens and markets from banking crises, energy spikes, or market crashes to avoid being voted out of office.
  • Currency Debasement Speculation: The certainty of this permanent safety net (the "Fed Put") encourages high-risk speculation while driving long-term currency debasement. This makes hard assets like Bitcoin highly attractive long-term debasement hedges.

Fed Leadership Transition: Powell Out, Warsh In

  • Jerome Powell’s Mixed Legacy: Powell's tenure was marked by massive crises, including the 2019 repo crisis, the pandemic, and historic inflation.
      • The Inflation Blindspot: Powell heavily damaged Fed credibility by mischaracterizing early inflation as "transitory."
      • Crypto Debanking: Powell was behind the regulatory pressure that led to the debanking of crypto firms and the eventual collapse of Silvergate Bank in March 2023.
  • Kevin Warsh's New Playbook: Incoming Fed Chair Kevin Warsh faces a challenging high-inflation environment.
      • Reduced Forward Guidance: Warsh wants to move away from "spoon-feeding" the market, which may result in fewer FOMC press conferences and dot plots, forcing analysts to reintroduce original thinking.
      • The Rate Outlook: Acheson predicts "no cuts and no hikes" in the immediate future. While a consensus has formed around no rate cuts, expectations of a rate hike are overblown given current economic uncertainties.

Crypto Markets: Coinbase-Hyperliquid Alliance and the Clarity Act

  • Coinbase and Hyperliquid's Strategic Alignment: Coinbase has significantly increased its position in staked HYPE, signaling deep institutional cooperation despite being natural competitors.
      • USDC Distribution Play: While Coinbase operates as a compliant, US-centric institution, it is leveraging Hyperliquid’s non-KYC, global perpetuals platform to grow international distribution of USDC.
      • Neutralizing Competition: For Coinbase and Circle, installing themselves as collateral managers for Hyperliquid is a highly strategic move to capture recurring net interest income.
  • The Clarity Act Catalyst: Acheson believes the Clarity Act has a reasonable chance of passing this year.
      • Ethereum and DeFi Benefit: While Bitcoin already possesses regulatory clarity, the bill would provide a massive regulatory unlock for Ethereum, staking, and decentralized autonomous organizations (DAOs).
      • Market Price-In: The regulatory progress is not fully priced into crypto markets due to a prolonged retail malaise, leaving room for a significant market jump if the bill is signed into law.

Notable Quotes

"The stock market is being driven... by intrinsic and temporary factors, excitement about AI mainly... whereas the bond market looks at the macro outlook... That's why [we have] two very different drummers, not painting the same story, but they don't need to." — Noelle Acheson
"The BLISS trade, which is 'Big, Large, and Sustained Stimulus' or support, is structural. Her [Gita Gopinath's] point is that there is no administration now that will not spend to get people out of difficulties... Governments are not going to be voted out of office because they are not spending to save people." — Noelle Acheson
"Inflation kills societies, and if we don't keep an eye on it, then there could be some very bad scenarios going ahead. Inflation will dictate monetary policy; inflation will have a very big impact on fiscal policy at a global level... You can massage the indices, but you can't hide from inflation." — Noelle Acheson

Well, the BLISS trade, for those who aren't familiar with it, stems from a really interesting opinion piece published in the Financial Times a couple weeks ago by, as you said, Steve, former IMF chief economist and deputy managing director Gita Gopinath, who's now a Harvard professor. And it was, again, part of—this has to be read through the lens of someone that comes from the IMF—but she makes the excellent point that the market assumption that there will be a put, if you will, or a safety net, is not to do with the TACO trade anymore. That's part of it for sure, and Trump certainly has given the market many things to hang the TACO expectation on, many things we want him to walk back, but her point is that this goes beyond that. The TACO trade is temporary; it's limited to Trump's term. But the BLISS trade, which is the acronym for "Big, Large, and Sustained Stimulus" or support, is structural. Her point is that there is no administration now that will not spend to get people out of difficulties, whatever those difficulties may be. Be it a market crash, be it a banking crisis, be it high energy prices. We saw this in 2020, we saw this in 2022 with the energy prices, and we're seeing this again, especially here in Europe anyway, with the Hormuz crisis. Governments are not going to be voted out of office because they are not spending to save people from whatever difficulty they may be in. Now, this is relevant for the outlook for currency debasement, obviously: where is the funding for the stimulus going to come from? Well, it's going to come from somewhere; they're going to simply make it happen through one of the many tools in their box. It's one of the reasons, I think, why we're just seeing so much risk-on appetite in this very uncertain environment, as you pointed out earlier. But it's structural—the put is now part of the system, and that of course will add a whole different layer of fragility. Yeah, and I'm really curious to see like when or if something might come to a head, because every time, anyone who's been trying to kind of predict the doom loop ends up missing out, because the market always recovers, often with like a like a K or a V or some type of letter that shows divergent returns. And while that has historically been the case, we can't assume that will always continue because, again, what is the basis of these earnings expectations? In many cases, it's the company guidance, which in many cases is just simply extrapolating demand. We're assuming a ton of demand for chips and for AI infrastructure that may or may not appear. It, again, it may—I'm not going to claim to be an AI expert here—but we've seen before, Steve, many times that technological innovations have their hype period in which the expectations are out of proportion to the reality and they do eventually suffer a correction. Maybe this is going to be the very first exception in history, possibly, but it's rash to bet everything you have on that. Notion’s Ivan Zhao: The Refounder

Sequoia Capital(204.0K)·63:07·4.4K views·2026-05-21
Ivan ZhaoFounder and CEO, Notion
TL;DRNotion co-founder and CEO Ivan Zhao and Sequoia's Brian Halligan discuss "jazz mode"—a decentralized, adaptive approach to organizational design that enables companies to run like improvisational bands rather than rigid marching bands.
By leveraging a barbell-shaped...

TL;DR

Notion co-founder and CEO Ivan Zhao and Sequoia's Brian Halligan discuss "jazz mode"—a decentralized, adaptive approach to organizational design that enables companies to run like improvisational bands rather than rigid marching bands. By leveraging a barbell-shaped engineering team, prioritizing "taste" and agency over experience, and executing radical "re-foundings," Notion demonstrates how legacy SaaS companies can successfully transition into fast-moving, AI-native enterprises.

"Jazz Mode" and Rethinking the Org Chart

  • The Jazz Band Philosophy: Ivan Zhao describes Notion’s operating model as a "jazz band, not a marching band." Instead of marching in a rigid, top-down formation, the organization relies on high autonomy, self-equilibrium, and individual improvisation to stay agile.
  • The Invariance of Hierarchy: While AI allows organizations to become flatter, Zhao argues that fully flat structures run contrary to human nature. He asserts that organizations will remain hierarchical because human beings are biologically wired for division of labor and hierarchical grouping.
  • The "Circle" Org Chart: Host Brian Halligan notes a structural shift from the traditional "triangle" org chart to a "circle" org chart. In this model, centralized AI systems sit in the middle, aggregating complete company context. Humans act as trainers feeding the system context and taste, allowing the AI to gradually automate micro-decision-making.
  • Leveraging Ex-Founders: Notion maintains a staff of roughly 50 to 60 ex-founders. This high density of entrepreneurial talent enables decentralized leadership, allowing team members to cross organizational boundaries and drive cross-functional projects without territorial friction.

Evolving Talent Profiles and the "Barbell" Engineering Team

  • The Barbell Team Structure: Rather than hiring mid-level talent, Notion has structured its engineering organization into a distinct barbell shape:
      • Super-Junior ICs: Energetic, high-agency, out-of-school individual contributors (ICs) who can scale their output rapidly using AI coding agents.
      • Super-Senior Architects: Highly experienced engineers who provide structural direction, systems design, and "taste"—skills that language models cannot replicate. They manage junior engineers, who in turn manage multiple coding agents.
  • Hiring for Taste and Will: Traditional hiring metrics prioritized "slope" (pure cognitive speed) and experience. Because AI has democratized and normalized capabilities like writing and programming, Notion now hires based on a different formula: $\text{Talent} = (\text{Capability/Experience}) \times \text{Taste} (Value System) \times \text{Will} (Agency/Work Ethic)$.
  • The Elimination of the CMO: Notion dismantled its traditional CMO organization, splitting it into two distinct, decentralized functions:
      • Storytelling: Placed directly adjacent to product and social media channels to match rapid product-shipping cadences.
      • Demand Generation: Aligned directly under sales to focus on lead generation and go-to-market execution.
  • No-Resume First Rounds: For sales hires, Notion's first-round evaluation bypasses traditional resumes. Candidates are instead required to physically build and submit something using Notion to demonstrate their hands-on product alignment.

The Art of "Re-founding"

  • Re-founding Twice: Zhao is a proponent of radical self-reinvention, having guided Notion through two crucial pivots:
      • Kyoto (2015): Lacking product-market fit and running out of cash, Zhao and co-founder Simon Last laid off their small team, sublet their San Francisco office to become cash-flow positive, and relocated to Kyoto, Japan. They spent a year and a half living together and rebuilding Notion's codebase from scratch.
      • Cancun (2023): Upon receiving early access to GPT-4—which Zhao described as a "full-body religious experience"—the team gathered in Mexico to pivot Notion into an AI-first company.
  • Decalcifying through M&A: To prevent organizational calcification as the company grew to over 1,000 employees, Notion intentionally used acquisitions and acquihires to bring in aggressive, external startup founders who challenge legacy internal thinking.
  • Transitioning to "Wartime": Zhao and Halligan agree that the SaaS industry has entered a "wartime" era. Financial planning remains conservative and metric-driven, but product planning is conducted week-by-week rather than on 3- or 6-month cycles to keep pace with rapid market changes.

Building with AI: Brewing Beer vs. Engineering Bridges

  • Software as Brewing Beer: Zhao notes that traditional software development is like "engineering a bridge" (highly predictable and design-led). Building with LLMs is like "brewing beer" (unpredictable, dependent on active experimentation, and reliant on throwing top talent at a model to see what flavor profiles emerge).
  • AI as "Organizational Steel": Zhao compares LLMs to structural steel in architecture. Before steel, buildings were limited to six stories; similarly, AI paired with software acts as "steel" for modern organizations, allowing them to scale taller and faster without being crushed by communication overhead.
  • Gross Margin Dynamics: While running AI products can negatively impact gross margins due to heavy GPU and token consumption, Zhao explains that knowledge-management platforms can mitigate this. Unlike coding products that require top-tier frontier models, knowledge tasks (e.g., filing IT tickets or search queries) can be effectively routed to cheaper, second-tier, or open-weight models.

The second kind of takeaway I had is, you know, I work with lots of startups and there are so many companies—let's say less than $500 million in revenue and growing like 30%—that are kind of stuck. It typically involves a big step back and two steps forward. It's going to be hard, and your numbers will look worse in the short term. Ivan didn't do this, but when we wanted to do a re-founding at HubSpot at one point, we started a new company inside of HubSpot that was completely independent from it, and that served us well. That's how Apple did it back in the day. But if you're stuck—and a lot of companies are stuck—be risk-on and do what Ivan did. He was a ye olde SaaS company, and now he's a rocket ship AI company. I think he's onto it. The last takeaway I have on Ivan kind of starts back in the podcast interview I did with Jack Dorsey a few weeks ago. It does seem to me there's a new way to build and run companies today that's a pretty big departure from the way people have done it the last 30 years. Jack's iterating on it, Ivan's iterating on it, and a lot of the, call it, vintage 2025 startups are iterating on it. This is something I'm thinking a lot about. I think there needs to be a new playbook on how to be a CEO in this AI-native era we're in. I'm early in that iteration, but roughly, the way I think about how they're organized is that, historically, there's a triangle-shaped org chart. Those org charts have been around a long, long time, since the Roman Empire. They're a very inefficient way to run a company, but they've been the best way because there's been no better alternative. I think the problem with them is, the bigger that org chart gets, the more the information gets distorted across, up, and down, and the slower it gets. So, it's pretty inefficient. The new way that Dorsey described—and I didn't quite get into it with Ivan, but I suspect it's similar—is a circle. In the middle is all of the context in the AI system. Initially, humans are making all the decisions. But over time, as you give that centralized AI system more context, it gets smarter. You feed it context, and you turn over more and more of the decisions to it. I don't think it happens overnight. I think it's quite gradual, but it's an IT task to get it right. You have to be legible—I don't love that word—but you have to have a legible set of systems, and you have to record everything. But I think this is the new way. Companies that are doing this seem to be moving a lot faster than the companies that aren't, and Ivan is moving at breakneck speed. I think there's a lot of things that fall out of it. They don't need as many people, they move much faster because of that, and they have much shorter planning cycles. All of those kind of "one-way doors" that Bezos used to talk about are really two-way doors because nothing takes that long to do. Anyway, I think there's this new playbook. I'm interested in it, and I'm going to be talking about it on the podcast and on X. If you're interested in it, too, hit me up at @bhalligan. Thanks for tuning in.

Beyond the keynote with Sundar Pichai

Google for Developers(2.6M)·31:42·2.3K views·2026-05-21
Sundar PichaiCEO, Google Matt BermanHost
TL;DRAt Google I/O 2026, Google CEO Sundar Pichai highlighted the industry's shift toward agentic workflows and the critical need for cost-efficient "workhorse" models like Gemini 3.5 Flash.
He emphasized that managing the rapid evolution of AI requires balancing open-source...

TL;DR

At Google I/O 2026, Google CEO Sundar Pichai highlighted the industry's shift toward agentic workflows and the critical need for cost-efficient "workhorse" models like Gemini 3.5 Flash. He emphasized that managing the rapid evolution of AI requires balancing open-source collaboration with responsible safety protocols while navigating systemic global compute bottlenecks.

The Rise of AI Agents and User Trust

  • Agentic Workflows: Developer-led workflows have evolved from basic code auto-completion to full agent orchestration.
      • During the event, Google demonstrated this capability in "Anti-Gravity" for building an operating system, proving that agents can handle highly complex, multi-step environments.
  • The Shared Trust Journey: Building trust in AI agents mirrors the societal adoption of email spam filters or self-driving cars like Waymo.
      • Deliberate Integration: To ensure safety, Google is slowly integrating its powerful Gemini Spark engine with first-party services (Gmail, Calendar) before exposing full browser use, computer use, and third-party integrations via the Model Context Protocol (MCP).
  • Automation vs. Delight: AI agents will increasingly handle tedious administrative "chores" (such as renewing a DMV license). This shift is intended to free up human users to focus on aspects of the internet that offer genuine exploration, joy, and direct connection with creators.

Cybersecurity and Model Deployment Paradigms

  • CodeMender Launch: Google introduced CodeMender, an internal tool now being deployed externally that continuously monitors for vulnerabilities, generates patches, tests them, and deploys updates 24/7 in real time.
  • Strategic Acquisitions: Google's integration of its Wiz acquisition provides state-of-the-art, real-time vulnerability monitoring to reinforce cloud infrastructure security.
  • Responsible Disclosure: Addressing frontier cyber-capable models (such as Anthropic's Mitheos and OpenAI's GPT-5.5 Cyber), Pichai advocated for a measured deployment strategy.
      • Google favors established security practices, such as its "Project Zero" framework, which gives vendors a 90-day window to patch vulnerabilities before public disclosure.
      • The Threshold for Release: Deployment decisions should be made on a model-by-model basis. A 1% to 2% improvement over the state-of-the-art warrants standard release, whereas 20% "generational jumps" require deep coordination with government and industry safety bodies.

Open Source Viability and Global Competition

  • The Gemma Ecosystem: Google remains committed to open source with its release of Gemma 4, but Pichai noted that the immense Capex and R&D costs required to build frontier models necessitate a balanced approach between open and closed architectures.
  • The Rise of Chinese Open Source: Regarding highly efficient Chinese models like DeepSeek, Pichai argued that enterprise clients select models based on reliability, predictability, and safety rather than geographic origin.
      • He emphasized that rather than fearing foreign open-source tech, the United States must focus on maintaining its own lead at the technological frontier.
  • Dynamic Software Architecture: Because AI technology is evolving rapidly, enterprises must design their technical stacks to be modular, allowing them to dynamically swap underlying models as the ecosystem shifts.

The Economics of AI: Workhorse Models and Compute Bottlenecks

  • Gemini 3.5 Flash: Designed specifically to curb ballooning enterprise AI budgets, the Flash class of models acts as a cost-efficient "workhorse."
      • High-Speed Efficiency: Flash is optimized for high-frequency agentic loops, with demos showing it running at speeds of up to 800 tokens per second.
  • The Compute Deficit: Google operates in a state of constant compute deficit, where demand heavily outstrips capacity. This scarcity forces continuous trade-offs between scaling massive, capability-maximizing "Ultra" models and serving efficient "Flash" models to billions of users.
  • Multilayered Bottlenecks: Pichai noted that solving one bottleneck simply exposes another. Google is actively managing parallel constraints across physical data center permitting, local power grid access, rising memory/component costs, and specialized silicon manufacturing.

Notable Quotes

"I do expect agents to be a core part of how we work... [but] it'll allow humans to use the internet in ways that give them joy and purpose and not always be forced to deal with 'I have to fill these 18 form fields to renew a DMV license.'" — Sundar Pichai
"The more AI becomes advanced, the more it's a societal conversation versus a single company conversation." — Sundar Pichai
"If you think something is a bottleneck and you solve it, something else becomes the bottleneck... there are systemic bottlenecks across all layers of the stack now." — Sundar Pichai

Having used them, you know, if you're living... maybe today the people who are on the frontier of how agents work are developers, right, particularly with coding. I don't know if other people agree with that, but there was almost an immediate trust that I had for Waymo. So I'm hoping agents are the same way. Sometimes it's very good because, you know, it helps improve user satisfaction because users are getting to what they want in a better way. And I think most companies should learn to use it that way. To be very clear, we're super committed to being at the frontier on every category. I'm excited for Pro which we are working on. But I think Flash has a unique role to play in this constrained, compute-constrained time. Yeah. No, I agree completely. Especially, most companies are not solving math olympiad problems, they're not at the absolute cutting edge of science. They need real work done and that is truly where the Flash model shines, and not everybody is token-maxing and unconcerned with the budget. So I definitely appreciate that. If you think about the future of AI, is it just truly a race to self-improving AI? Look, I think, first of all, I mean, there's a responsibility that comes with this technology and, you know, I think we all need to be careful to avoid this race condition at all costs. And, you know, I think we owe it to humanity to make sure we deploy this technology responsibly. You are right in your question that there is this current moment where people feel like the curve is so steep and, you know, where you are in the curve matters. But just like a few months ago when we launched 3.0, people are like, "Oh, we are so in the frontier, no one will ever be able to catch up," and you know... I think at the frontier labs, it's very dynamic; the competition is fierce. We all have our strengths and weaknesses. We all also have different cadences of our pre-training release cycles. So the peaks don't exactly match. So all this creates that perception gap which shifts widely in like 4 to 6 weeks. But I think a few labs are really at the frontier and then there's a big gap. And I think there are scenarios in which things like recursive self-improvement come into play. But I think if they come into play, with that, no difference from the cyber moment, we all have to handle those moments far more responsibly than today. And so that goes hand-in-hand. And so I think the more AI becomes advanced, the more it's a societal conversation versus a single company conversation. Yeah. Well said. So all of... you know, we're talking a lot about models, but all of it is downstream from compute. I'm always both impressed and in awe of Google's ability to serve... you're serving your own models, inference on the API, you're also powering your suite of products with Gemini to literally billions of users, you're also allowing your competitors to use your inference, you're also selling TPUs. I've heard the reason for this is because Thomas told me, he said, "We planned really well," which, you know, makes a lot of sense; you've been at this for 10 plus years. I think I've also heard that Google's revenue is literally constrained by compute. So I wanted to give you the opportunity: what is the state of compute at Google right now? Look, I... all of us are... I think we have made a set of, you know, bold, right, decisions over the last few years to invest in compute and scale it up aggressively. But having said that, I don't think any of us sit in the chair and say, "We wish we had..." You know, you look back and say, "I wish I'd done a little bit more." So we're living in one of those moments in time. And, you know, there are costs going up too, so like for a given budget, you may be getting less compute than you had previously planned for. Memory prices, what have you—you know, so the costs are going up. I think we plan... you know, we are able to plan long-term for Cloud separately from our own internal needs and we do long-range plans and we plan for it. I think it's good, and some of it is like when you're on Google Cloud and you're supporting customers, your customers may look at something and say, "Well, I want access to that." Like, they may look at the demo of 3.5 Flash on Anti-Gravity and say, "How are you exactly running it at 800 tokens per second, and like, could we get access to that?" You know, so you're also supporting customers through those journeys and hence you're meeting them in what they are asking for. And it is not an easy balancing act and, you know, we are constantly thinking as far ahead as possible and we are making trade-offs, like every other company right now. And so do you have, maybe an obvious question, more demand than you have compute to serve it? Absolutely. And hence the emphasis on something like 3.5 Flash even more. Right? And you know, could we have done an even better Omni model? Yes, but like, how can we do an Omni model which we can give to as many people as possible? So there's constant... you're making trade-offs of trade-offs of like that. Including on, you know, do you build a very large model, which, you know, like an ultra-sized model that will increase the capability frontier, but then who all can you give it to? So, constantly, all of us are making these trade-offs and sometimes you make a trade-off and it looks like, "Well, maybe you've done the trade-off a bit differently." So we only have a few seconds left. One last question for you. What is the main bottleneck for compute for Google right now? Is it land? Is it political will? What is it? I think the way it works is, by definition, bottlenecks work this way: if you think something is a bottleneck and you solve it, something else becomes the bottleneck. Right? Like, that's the whole definition of bottleneck. I think at various times you are having a few areas. Your ability to physically permit and construct data centers, the power they need, and then very quickly you get into the core components for these systems. They are all the bottleneck and it's like, you need all of that to work together to get a square set of a chip you need, right? And like, you know. And so I feel like there are a few parallel bottlenecks going through and it almost doesn't matter, there are like a few of them which are bottlenecks. And at various times you may conclude it is memory, but then if everyone concludes it's memory, tomorrow you turn around and say, "Okay, no, no, no, it's actually this." And so, you know, but I think there are systemic bottlenecks across all layers of the stack now. Whatever the bottleneck is in the moment, that's the most compute you can have in that moment. Okay. Everybody, please help me thank Sundar Pichai. Thank you so much. Thank you so much. Appreciate it. Thank you.

Sundar Pichai on Agents Replacing Engineers, Google's Future, AI's Flip Phone Moment, and More

Rowan Cheung(172.0K)·20:17·9.3K views·2026-05-21
Sundar PichaiCEO, Alphabet and Google Rowan CheungFounder, The Rundown AI
TL;DRGoogle CEO Sundar Pichai highlights a rapid shift toward proactive, personalized AI agents that will make today’s technology look primitive within three years, emphasizing that Google’s strategy remains deeply "creator-first" and focused on…
enhancing human productivity rather...

TL;DR

Google CEO Sundar Pichai highlights a rapid shift toward proactive, personalized AI agents that will make today’s technology look primitive within three years, emphasizing that Google’s strategy remains deeply "creator-first" and focused on enhancing human productivity rather than replacing human connection.

***

The "Flip Phone" Moment and the Shift to AI Agents

  • The Three-Year Outlook: Pichai predicts that the pace of AI advancement is on a steep curve; in three years, today's cutting-edge AI models will be viewed as "primitive," akin to looking back at a flip phone.
  • Proactive, Cross-Device Intelligence:
      • Continuous Context: Google is building personal intelligence that runs 24/7 in Google Cloud, syncing context across laptops, phones, and watches.
      • User Control: To balance this deep personalization, Google is implementing strict user controls, including incognito modes and the ability to pause agent activity.
  • The Shift to Agentic Action: With platforms like Spark, the value of AI is shifting from static benchmarks to "agentic" capabilities—taking on complex, multi-step real-world tasks (e.g., planning a trip, organizing weekly groceries) to free up human time for more meaningful experiences.
  • Fluid Modality: The future of content consumption will allow any input modality (text, audio, video) to seamlessly translate to any other modality, fundamentally changing how users interact with information.

YouTube’s "Creator-First" AI Strategy

  • Human-to-Human Priority: In contrast to competitors aiming for fully AI-generated social feeds, Pichai emphasizes that YouTube’s strategy remains firmly "creator-first." The core value of the platform lies in the authentic connection between creators and their audiences.
  • Creative Force Multipliers: Generative tools like Veo, Omni, and YouTube's Flow are designed to democratize high-end production. Pichai compares this shift to the introduction of CGI, noting it will allow independent creators—even high school students—to produce movie-quality content.
  • Navigating Creator Concerns:
      • Protecting Watch Time: Regarding features like "Ask YouTube" (which allows viewers to query and compare videos), Google is testing carefully to ensure these tools drive deeper engagement and pull more complex search queries into the ecosystem rather than cannibalizing creator watch time.
      • Creator Education: Acknowledging that some creators fear audience backlash for using AI, Pichai highlighted the need for better education on tools like auto-dubbing, which dramatically expand a creator's global reach.

The Future of Software Engineering and Agentic Coding

  • Beyond Auto-Completion: While 75% of new code at Google is currently generated with AI assistance, Pichai states that simple code completion is no longer the true frontier.
  • Orchestrating Teams of Agents: The role of the software engineer is shifting toward agent management and orchestration. Rather than just writing lines of code, engineers will manage fleets of specialized sub-agents to execute complex, long-running tasks.
  • Automated Code Migration: Google is already utilizing "agentic coding" internally to tackle massive infrastructure projects, such as deploying agent fleets to migrate complex codebases between languages (e.g., converting C++ to Rust, or Python to Go) using the existing binary as the technical specification.

Navigating Rapid Change: Advice for the Next Generation

  • Managing Technological Anxiety: Pichai acknowledges that humans are not naturally wired for the current velocity of technological change.
  • Continuous Learning Mindset: He advises young people to focus on continuous adaptation, treating AI tools as personal learning accelerators—similar to how previous generations used YouTube to self-educate.
  • The Enduring Value of College: Despite the rise of self-directed digital learning, Pichai still recommends attending college for its role in personal growth, developing deep social networks, and fostering critical life skills.

***
