How to Build a Self-Improving Company with AI

Y Combinator(2.3M)·13:29·27.5K views·2026-05-21
Tom BlomfieldGeneral Partner, Y Combinator
TL;DRInstead of using AI as a mere productivity "copilot" within traditional corporate hierarchies, founders should design their companies as a series of autonomous, recursive AI loops that self-improve overnight by operating on fully recorded, legible organizational knowledge.

TL;DR

Instead of using AI as a mere productivity "copilot" within traditional corporate hierarchies, founders should design their companies as a series of autonomous, recursive AI loops that self-improve overnight by operating on fully recorded, legible organizational knowledge.

Beyond Copilots: Reimagining the Company Structure

  • The Roman Legion Fallacy: Traditional corporate structures operate like Roman legions—nested hierarchies where humans serve as the primary conduits for passing orders down and information up. AI fundamentally breaks this assumption.
  • The Copilot Misconception: Treating AI as a "copilot" to make employees 20% more productive is a flawed approach. It merely bolts a faster engine onto an obsolete way of working.
  • Extracting Domain Knowledge: The true power of AI lies in centralizing and extracting "know-how" from Slack messages, emails, Notion, and recordings, and packaging it into a unified context or set of skills that an AI agent can autonomously act upon.

The Recursive, Self-Improving AI Loop

  • The Five-Layer Autonomous Loop: To build a system that improves while you sleep, companies must link five core layers without human bottlenecks:
      • Sensor Layer: Captures real-world inputs (customer emails, support tickets, cancellations, product telemetry).
      • Policy/Decision Layer: Governs rules, guardrails, and parameters for when the AI must ask a human for permission.
      • Tool Layer: Deterministic APIs and code execution (e.g., querying databases, checking calendars).
      • Quality Gate: Evaluates safety filters, deterministic checks, and human review for high-risk actions.
      • Learning Mechanism: Analyzes failures and successes to feed back into the top of the loop.
  • The "Holy Shit" YC Milestone: YC built an agent to query its internal database. They added a monitoring agent that reviewed every failed search, identified missing indexes or code, wrote a merge request to the YC codebase, had an AI agent review it, and deployed it overnight. When employees logged in the next day, the system had autonomously fixed its own software bugs.
  • Self-Optimizing Business Functions:
      • *Product Analytics:* AI agents can continuously review sales funnels, identify friction points, design and run A/B tests, and deploy the winning version.
      • *Customer Support to Code:* An AI agent acting as "CPO/CTO" can triage user feedback, identify features aligned with the roadmap, write the code, and ship it to production overnight without human intervention.

Structural and Operational Implications

  • Burn Tokens, Not Headcount: YC startups are reaching Demo Day with roughly 5x more revenue per employee than they did 18 months ago.
      • *Token Maxing:* Startups should measure and optimize for token usage rather than headcount.
  • The End of Middle Management: Coordination-heavy middle management roles are no longer necessary. Effective organizations will consist of only two core human roles:
      • *Individual Contributors (ICs):* Hands-on builders and operators.
      • *Directly Responsible Individuals (DRIs):* A single, named human (not a committee) accountable for driving specific outcomes.

Legibility and Ephemeral Software

  • Making the Organization Legible: To leverage AI, every email, Slack DM, and partner conversation must be recorded, transcribed, and diarized.
      • *The Rule of Legibility:* "If it did not get recorded, it did not happen to your intelligence."
  • The Living YC User Manual: YC General Partner Harj took 2,000 hours of recorded office hours from a three-month period, diarized and categorized the transcripts, and used AI to generate a brand-new, 150-page YC User Manual over a single weekend. This manual now self-improves dynamically as new office hours are recorded.
  • Software as an Ephemeral Asset: With advanced code generation (e.g., Codex 55), internal dashboards and software tools should be treated as entirely disposable.
      • *Data Preservation vs. Software Ephemerality:* Companies should meticulously preserve business data and context but generate, throw away, and regenerate the software interfaces on top of them as underlying AI models improve.

Where Humans Still Matter

  • Humans on the Edge: In a self-improving company, the AI functions as the central "company brain" while humans operate on the periphery, interfacing with the real world where models cannot yet reach.
  • High-Stakes and Emotional Contexts: Human intervention remains critical for:
      • Novel, unmapped situations and ethical considerations.
      • High-stakes, high-emotion scenarios (such as resolving co-founder disputes).
      • B2B sales and relationship-building conversations.

Notable Quotes

"AI isn't something you bolt onto the side of a company... I think you can reimagine what a company is as a set of recursive, self-improving AI loops." — Tom Blomfield
"If it is recorded, it happened to the AI. If it did not get recorded, it did not happen to your intelligence." — Tom Blomfield
"I think the business context and skills are the valuable part. I think the software on top of it is ephemeral." — Tom Blomfield
This is based a little bit off a talk Diana gave; there's a video up over the weekend which is super cool. Jack Dorsey was tweeting some stuff like two or three weeks ago that I thought was super cool. And I've kind of stolen a bunch of those ideas and shoved them into here. This talk is pretty conceptual and high-level about thinking about how to build companies. The Roman legions were designed to project power over two continents or something, from Rome at the center to people on Hadrian's Wall up in Scotland. The idea was these nested hierarchies with consistent spans of control. You had named individuals with spans of control to pass orders down and send information back up the hierarchy. If you think about most companies today, they are organized like a Roman legion, where human beings are the conduit for information flowing up and down. And so Jack Dorsey's tweet, which I thought was great, was about this underlying assumption that hierarchically organized companies are the way that we should be organizing our economic units of value. I think AI basically breaks that. If you talked to people a year ago about how AI was useful, they talked about productivity—like co-pilots making engineers 20% more productive, adding co-pilots to workflows, and shipping more software. But I think that is actually a broken way of thinking about AI. That's like—Pete had a great blog post where basically you're just taking the old way of working and adding a more powerful engine onto it. Instead of that, I think you can reimagine what a company is and how it acts. As Gary was talking, I genuinely believe he can produce more code than an entire engineering team. The thing that's really stuck with me is this idea of extracting the domain knowledge from your company and defining it as a context, a set of skills, or whatever you want to call it. There is domain knowledge, business knowledge, or some know-how that's inside the heads of people, and in Slack messages, and in emails, and in Notion. All of this information together defines how your company works. If you can make that legible, you suddenly can move from this hierarchical organization to a sort of intelligent, AI-powered organization with AI-native software. AI isn't something you bolt onto the side of a company; it's not like a tool you give to your engineers to make them more productive. I think you can reimagine what a company is as a set of recursive, self-improving AI loops. I think this is really, really, really important, because when it gets there, I think the company starts to self-improve even when you're sleeping. So let me give you an example—Diana's talk talks about this as well. With this AI loop, you start with a sensor layer, which is a fancy word, but really it might be emails from your customers, support tickets, code changes, people canceling their subscription, or product telemetry. It's like sensor data to get information from the outside world. Then you have a policy layer or decision layer, with rules about what you can do, what it has to ask a human permission for, and what it must log. Next is a tool layer—that's kind of Gary's skills and code. The tool layer is basically deterministic APIs, things like "query my database" or "look at my calendar"—a set of tools that the AI can call. Then there is a quality gate, which might be evals, deterministic checks, safety filters, or human review for high-risk stuff. And then a learning mechanism. Your system interacts with the real world, picks up where it doesn't work, and loops back into the top again. If you can run every single step of that without human intervention, or with minimal human intervention, your system gets better and better and better while you're sleeping. I can give you actual examples of this that are live right now. We started with an agent that you can ask, and it has deterministic tools to query our database—pretty simple, like, "When did I last have office hours with this company?" Then it got a little bit smarter, which was like, "For this company I'm doing office hours with right now, they need introductions to anyone in petrochemicals," or something. It could query the database in different ways and use RAG and all sorts of stuff to come up with five relevant founders for you to meet. But again, this is like a sidekick, right? This is an agent. This is like last year's version of how AI is making me better as a group partner, making me 20% or 30% more effective. The aha moment for me came when we put a monitoring agent on top of that, which looked at every single query every single YC employee was doing and saw when it worked and when it did not work. And when it did not work, it was like, "Oh, why not? What would have made this query work? Do we need different deterministic tools? Do we need to update the skills file? Do we need a different database view? Do we need a new index?" This literally happens overnight now. It will write the code, put in a merge request with the YC codebase, have an agent review it, merge it, and deploy it. So when a human comes the next day to ask the same query, it will now succeed. For me, that was like the "holy fucking shit" moment. Right, that's not just AI making you 20% or 30% more valuable; it is the AI going through this loop to figure out how to self-improve. I think basically if you can identify parts of your company that work like this, and eliminate as much human labor as possible to have the human in a monitoring or supervisory capacity, you can just throw tokens at this problem and your company will get better. Other examples might be if you have product analytics: having an agent go through your product analytics to figure out what part of your sales funnel is presenting the highest amount of friction, researching best practices, putting in place an A/B test, running it for a week, picking the best version, and deploying it. And you just do that again and again and again for your product, so you have a self-optimizing product loop. Or you do it with customer service queries. You have customer suggestions coming in constantly, and you triage them. You can have an agent acting like your Chief Product Officer and your Chief Technology Officer who make judgment calls: "Okay, this is a suggestion we just don't want to do, we'll discard it," or "No, this is a suggestion which is now in line with our roadmap, we can do it overnight. Let's write the code, deploy it, and ship it to the customer without a human being involved." So I think if you can think about each part of your company as a self-improving, recursive AI loop, it becomes very, very different from this hierarchically organized Roman legion of a company. So, if you want to do this, what are the implications? One is: burn tokens, not headcount. We are seeing companies get to Demo Day with about 5x more revenue per employee than they did 18 months ago. I think that's going to continue to Series A and Series B. And so, I think you're going to be constrained on token usage, not on headcount, really, really soon. The current measure now is just measuring everyone's token usage, which is obviously dumb and gameable at the extreme, but directionally I think it is correct. We're in the phase of discovering what is possible right now, and so everyone should be experimenting to the max to figure out what we can even do with this crazy new intelligence we have. As soon as you turn it into a leaderboard and people get promoted or fired based on it, obviously it gets gamed, and obviously that's dumb. But I think directionally figuring out who in your organization is token maxing and who is not is a good way to think about which employees you should be spending your time with. I think middle management is done. I just don't think you need middle management for this coordination problem; I think AI should be doing it. For me, there are two roles—Jack Dorsey has three, but I actually don't like the third one, so I deleted it—but there are two roles that really, really matter to me. I think everyone just has to be an IC now: a builder, an operator. And I think crucially, to get anything done, you need directly responsible individuals—you need a named human. Not a committee, not a group of people, just a single person. I think you can build companies based on ICs, effectively. I think middle management is over. So, building this self-improving company—that's the dream. And by the way, I think people are at the bleeding edge of this right now. I'd be interested to see where you all are, but it feels like people are exploring the boundaries here. I'm not sure anyone has a truly self-improving company in every function. I might be wrong; you might prove me wrong. What would I do? First of all, this is really, really important: I would make the entire organization legible to AI. What does that mean? It means you've got to record everything. Simplistically, all of our partner emails now—if you email a YC partner, that email is in the YC database. Every Slack message, every DM, and every office hour, we've started recording for the last three or four months. Every single thing that happens, if it is recorded, it happened to the AI. If it did not get recorded, it did not happen to your intelligence, you know what I mean? And so, I was talking with some founders over here just now, and we were having really good conversations about their company. But during every conversation I had, I was like, "Fuck, I need to be recording this conversation," because some guy wanted an introduction—and I can't even remember who the introduction was for now. Who was that? I was talking to someone and I promised an introduction. I said yes, and then I said, "Email me afterwards," because I'm going to forget this after talking to 20 people. Yeah, so it needs to be on my phone, or a clip, or smart glasses, or we need to deck out every room with microphones. Basically, everything needs to be recorded so that it can be legible to the AI. And then, as Gary talked about, you have diarization. You cannot pump 100,000 hours worth of recordings into a context window, so you have to diarize it. You have to basically aggregate it down, synthesize it into the important parts, and then give the AI breadcrumbs. Okay, so here's an example: Who's read the user manual? The YC user manual? Hopefully everyone in this room has at least opened the user manual at one point in time, right? It's fine, but most of it was written 5 to 10 years ago. It's kind of out of date. So Harj thought last weekend, "Since now we've got about 2,000 hours of recorded office hours from the last 3 months, why don't we regenerate the user manual?" You give it a set of instructions, basically diarize it down, synthesize and categorize it into certain areas—like fundraising, hiring, co-founder disputes, whatever—and then tell it, "Write me a new user manual." By the end of the weekend, he had a 150-page user manual, which is dramatically better than the existing user manual. And now, we can also update it every single month. So, our user manual becomes self-improving. Every new piece of advice we give is compared with the existing user manual and either incorporated or thrown away. The user manual becomes this up-to-date, living brain of the advice we give to founders. Obviously, it doesn't stop as a user manual. You then pump it in as context to an AI agent, and suddenly you can ask a superintelligent AI and get the combined wisdom of 16 YC partners in one. But only if it's legible. So, you have to record everything. The second point is kind of the same, right? If it creates an artifact that can self-improve, it's legible; if it doesn't, you throw it away. The third point, then, is that every function can generate—this used to say dashboards, but it's not just dashboards, it's on-demand software. Codex 55 is now good enough that you can one-shot most simple internal software dashboards to a pretty high level of quality. I tried it over the weekend on a bunch of our stuff, and it's just unreal. So, all of your internal operations teams should be sitting on this layer of intelligence understanding, and then creating their own dashboards and their own workflows. And I would see those as entirely disposable. I would very preciously store all the data—as Gary said, he puts all of his emails in markdown and never throws anything away—but then treat the software as ephemeral. You can generate it, and you can regenerate it. The valuable part is the comprehension inside people's heads, like, "This is how the function works, this is how we run a YC event," whatever. The software to actually run the event, you can generate for the event and then throw away. When the models get smarter in a month or two, throw the software away, give it your original set of instructions, and regenerate the software. So, I think the business context and skills are the valuable part. I think the software on top of it is ephemeral. So, what are humans for in this world? I think basically we're talking about a company brain. I know a bunch of people in this room are building this, but the bit in the middle—all of your data, your emails, your DMs, the skills, the know-how—that is the company brain. And I think the humans sit around the edge of this, interfacing with the real world. It's where this intelligence makes contact with reality. Human beings reach into places the models can't go yet. That might be a conference, or it might be—I'm trying to think of examples, I would say a phone call, but I think AI can reach into phone calls pretty easily now. I think it's more like novel situations, ethical considerations, and high-stakes moments, you know? It's where the founder comes to us and is thinking about breaking up with their co-founder. It's those real high-stakes, high-emotion moments where you really want a human being. I think that's where the human fits. For all of you, like with sales conversations, I think that's a human being in the room for the next 20 years. So, the humans live, I think, around the edge. And I'm over time, and Harj is going to bullhorn me. I will leave you with one question: If you were building your company today, would you start it in this shape? For most of you, you're small enough to build it right, so I don't think you have any excuse. And I know there are a few of you who are in the process of ripping up and rebuilding your company. So, with that, I will stop and hand over to Pete. Thank you for listening.

A new era of discovery: AI and the frontiers of science with Demis Hassabis

Google for Developers(2.6M)·42:48·16.4K views·2026-05-21
Demis HassabisCo-founder and CEO, Google DeepMind Mike AllenCo-founder, Axios
TL;DRGoogle DeepMind CEO Demis Hassabis predicts that Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) will arrive between 2029 and 2031, ushering in a transformative "singularity" era marked by self-improving code, agentic systems, and a massive 100x acceleration of scientific and medical discovery.

TL;DR

Google DeepMind CEO Demis Hassabis predicts that Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) will arrive between 2029 and 2031, ushering in a transformative "singularity" era marked by self-improving code, agentic systems, and a massive 100x acceleration of scientific and medical discovery.

The Path and Timeline to AGI

  • AGI Arrival Horizon: Hassabis projects AGI will be realized around 2030 (specifically within the 2029–2031 window).
  • The "Einstein Test" for AGI: While current models show "jagged intelligence," true AGI will require a leap of conceptual insight.
    • *The Benchmark:* Hassabis defines this as giving a model a knowledge cutoff of 1901 and evaluating whether it can independently formulate the conceptual leaps Albert Einstein did in 1905, such as special relativity.
  • Feeling the Shift: Hassabis notes the approach of AGI is already palpable. He cites "vibe coding" game prototypes in his spare time in just 1–2 hours—tasks that previously required up to six months of manual engineering.
  • The Singularity: Hassabis describes this impending era as the most transformative invention in human history, making long-term predictions beyond its arrival point nearly impossible.

Google's Edge in the "Ferocious" AI Race

  • The Full-Stack Advantage: Hassabis asserts Google is the only organization in the world operating a complete feedback loop across the entire AI pipeline:
    • Proprietary custom chips (TPUs) and specialized data centers.
    • Scaled cloud infrastructure.
    • A world-class frontier research bench (Google DeepMind).
    • Direct distribution channels reaching billions of consumers.
  • Rapid Scale and Adoption: Google's streamlined, "agent-first" tech stack has allowed it to deploy powerful, efficient models directly to massive user bases.
    • *Milestones:* The Gemini App now boasts 900 million monthly active users, alongside massive deployments of AI Overviews in Search.
  • A Broad Research Bench: Hassabis points to Google's diverse research capabilities—developing media generation models like Omni and highly efficient, performant models like Gemini 1.5 Flash and Pro—as its ultimate competitive edge.

Societal Impact, "100x" Acceleration, and the Industry Backlash

  • The 100x Industrial Revolution: Hassabis estimates AI's impact will be 10 times greater than the Industrial Revolution and occur 10 times faster (occurring over a decade rather than a century).
  • Democratization of Opportunity: AI tools lower the barrier to creation, allowing a single motivated individual or a 1-to-2-person startup to build what once required a team of 50 specialized software engineers.
  • Addressing Public Backlash: Hassabis believes the AI industry must combat growing public anxiety and "tech backlash" by prioritizing and demonstrating unequivocal, concrete societal benefits.
    • *Key Areas:* The main counterweight to public skepticism is applying AI to hard science challenges, such as drug discovery, climate mitigation, materials science, and clean energy.
  • A Cautious Optimist Philosophy: Rejecting extreme positions of both "doomers" and pure "accelerationists," Hassabis argues for taking a measured approach to AGI. He emphasizes that society must give itself the time to implement safety, security, and robust guardrails over the next few years.

AI for Science: From AlphaFold to World Models

  • The AlphaFold Paradigm Shift: Hassabis recounts a pivotal decision filmed for the documentary *The Thinking Game*. Instead of setting up a standard web portal where scientists submitted individual genetic sequences for protein-folding predictions, DeepMind chose to run every known protein in existence (over 200 million) and release the entire database for free to the global scientific community.
  • Simulations and "Genie": Simulations are critical for both teaching AI systems and generating safe training data.
    • *Real-World Testing:* Google is utilizing "Genie"—an interactive 3D world model—to safely simulate extreme, "one-in-a-billion" edge cases for Waymo autonomous vehicles (e.g., simulating forest fires or wild animals on the road).
    • *Complex Systems:* DeepMind's weather forecasting models can predict extreme events, like Hurricane Beryl, much faster and more accurately than traditional fluid dynamics equations.
  • The Next Technical Step-Changes: Leading labs are heavily investing in several critical evolutionary steps for models:
    • *Self-Improvement:* Developing coding and mathematics agents where outputs are easily verifiable, creating a self-reinforcing flywheel.
    • *Continuous Learning:* Moving past static post-training cutoffs so models can adapt and learn dynamically after release.
    • *Consistency:* Eliminating "jagged" performance profiles so models do not fail on simple, out-of-distribution variations of tasks they otherwise excel at.

Builder Advice: Direction over Velocity

  • The Warning on Freneticism: While praising the unparalleled builder energy of Silicon Valley, Hassabis cautions that extreme "freneticism" can inhibit deep, long-term scientific thinking.
  • The Velocity Vector: Hassabis warns developers and founders against favoring speed over direction.
    • *Reflective Thinking:* For deep-tech breakthroughs that take years to yield results, ensuring the correct strategic direction is far more critical than raw speed.

Notable Quotes

"When we look back at this time, I think we will realize we were standing in the foothills of the singularity." — Sir Demis Hassabis
"If you want to try and quantify it, it’s going to be 10 times the impact of the Industrial Revolution, maybe 10 times faster... So you can think of it as 100x the Industrial Revolution, and that might be an underestimate." — Sir Demis Hassabis
"Making sure you have the direction right is often more important than just the speed... If you’re running 100 miles an hour in the wrong direction, that’s actually worse than standing still and taking a moment to think about getting the direction right." — Sir Demis Hassabis
I'm Mike Allen, co-founder of Axios. Thank you to the Google I/O team for making these conversations possible. Demis Hassabis started as a chess savant and a gamer, and is now the co-founder and CEO of Google DeepMind, where he's on the front lines of both the scientific race and the business race for AI. DeepMind's landmark AI breakthroughs include AlphaGo and AlphaFold, which solved the 50-year grand challenge of protein structure. For cracking that code, Demis was awarded the Lasker Award, which is the precursor to the Nobel Prize in Chemistry. The Economist last month put Demis on the cover as a Greek god, complete with tunic, in a pantheon of the titans who are shaping all of our future. He's even been knighted. The mind behind DeepMind, Sir Demis Hassabis, welcome to Dialogues. "Hey, Mike. Good to see you." "Thank you so much for doing this." "Oh, thanks for doing it." So, Demis, yesterday at your keynote, you got, by the way, big applause and even whoops for announcing Gemini for Science, which is one of your favorites. But you left us with the ultimate cliffhanger. You said, "When we look back at this time, I think we will realize we were standing in the foothills of the singularity." Take us one layer deeper. What do you think is going to happen? What do you hope is going to happen? Yeah, I thought people seemed to pick up on that line specifically. So, it was kind of expected, but it's great to be able to expand on it a little bit. I've been talking a lot about, obviously, AGI being on the horizon. I think we are only a few years away now from the full version of that. And the singularity is a kind of good word, I think, to describe the era that is precipitated by a technology like AGI arriving on the scene. Partly, it's because it's going to be so transformative—I think the most important invention ever—that it's hard to make many predictions beyond that horizon, because it will change so much. And I think this year, with the agentic systems that we're all seeing and using, we can start feeling it now. That's what I was sort of meaning by that. When we look back, I think, in 5 to 10 years' time, we'll sort of see it like, "Oh yes, 2026, 2027 is sort of when it was starting." And I love that: "We're starting to feel it." Expand on that a little bit. How do you feel it? Well, I think all of you—I mean, I've been feeling it just by using our own coding systems and coding agents, and just vibe coding things, even like little game prototypes in my spare one or two hours in the small hours of the morning, which would have taken, you know, six months before. I just think that kind of acceleration is very interesting to see. I think we're seeing it in areas of science, obviously with AlphaFold, which was a few years ago now, but almost every branch of science is having AI applied to it. And I'm also seeing the speed at which we're making advances in things like Isomorphic Labs for drug discovery. So, I think taking that all together—looking at the current progress of the foundation models, the chatbot systems, and things like Omni—all of that taken together is just astounding progress, I would say. Yeah, and based on the markers you set over the years, I feel like we're kind of right on schedule. You say a few years; what is your latest marker? Well, I feel like, you know, I've been saying recently around 2030, plus or minus a year, is a kind of reasonable estimate from what I'm seeing now. So, 2029 to 2031. Mhm. DeepMind's original mission statement was, "Solve intelligence." How are we doing? Well, I think we're doing pretty well. Our original statement was, you know, step one, solve intelligence; step two, use it to solve everything else. And I think that was pretty prescient. That's what we felt AGI would be: a general-purpose technology that could help—I was thinking more about science and medicine specifically—increasing our understanding of how the world works around us. That's always been my passion, as you know, and I think we are on the cusp of that now. In the lifetime of the builders and searchers in this room and watching us around the world, you think intelligence will be solved? Yes. Well, I mean, look, "solved" is just a neat term. What does it actually mean? But what I meant by that was building AGI, and I think, you know, we're a few years away. So, that would be the first step to me. And you think with AGI, different people talk about it different ways, but I think you're one of the people that thinks there will be a moment that we will know. Well, no, I think that's not the case. I think it's going to be a gradual improvement—that's what we're seeing, probably a pretty fast improvement. But I have a particular test in mind, which I call the Einstein test. Imagine you had a knowledge cutoff of one of these leading frontier models in 1901. Would it be able to come up with his incredible insights and discoveries that he did in 1905, including special relativity? Could a system come up with those insights and breakthroughs? Today's systems clearly can't, but I don't see why in the future they won't be able to. So, that's the sort of creativity or leap of insight that I'm still looking for from these systems for it to be full AGI. There is lots of buzz among tech writers in the press park, right here at Google I/O, about Google having its groove back. Brian Chen of the New York Times posted yesterday how Google is starting to win the AI race. If you win, why will it be? What is the Google edge, and what changed? Look, I think it's sort of weird, this idea of like, "We're back," and then, "We're winning," and then, "We're not back." The thing is that, at least among the three frontier labs—and actually the whole of the frontier—it's the most ferocious competition there's ever been, I would say, in tech history, maybe in corporate history. There are very capable, brilliant people in all the organizations, each trying to push as hard as they can on the research and the breakthroughs. I think we're doing really well for what we need to do, both in terms of the underlying technologies. We have a broader research bench than I think the other labs have; we've always had that. I think that's what gives us our innovative capacity to invent the next breakthroughs. You've seen our new models that are coming out—our 1.5 Flash, and Pro coming soon. We're very happy with the progress that we're making on the underlying models. We also have all of our generative media models, Omni being the latest, so that's going well. Where I think things are going extremely well is on the consumer side. Applying—I think that's what the really big leap has been for us in the last year—is streamlining our tech stack, actually almost rewriting it from the ground up to be AI-first, and now agent-first. That's a big change for an incredible engineering organization like Google that serves billions of users every day on multiple product surfaces with our incredible engineering stack. But that has to adapt so that you can ship the goodness of the latest models directly into all of those products at massive, massive scale. And I think we've got that down really well now, better than any of the other, shall we say, big tech companies. You're starting to see that in the progress we're making with the adoption of the Gemini App—you know, 900 million monthly users now, incredible growth there that we're seeing. And also things like AI Overviews in Search, which are unbelievably popular and an incredibly useful version of AI for information seeking. So, I think when you look at that in aggregate, and then you also see the advantage we have being full-stack—all the way from the chips to the data centers, to cloud, to the frontier lab, to billion-user products that have AI embedded in them—we are the only organization in the world that has that full stack. We're continually trying to optimize that feedback loop so that they all feed into each other. Do you think you will win? Well, I think we're going to be one of the big winners of the era. How many winners will there be? Well, I don't know. I don't really see it like that in terms of, you know, winning—win what, exactly? I think the AI era is going to be immense. There is going to be an unbelievable amount of new opportunities created and new economic progress made. We've got to make sure that we're one of the key players in that new post-AGI world, and I think we're incredibly well-positioned for that. As to how many players there will be, my guess is a handful, given how powerful the technology is. But actually, stepping back from that, what I always say is, although we're ferociously competing in this race, all the leaders of the frontier labs need to think about—and they are thinking about—the bigger picture of our responsibility to the world. As we bring this unbelievable technology into the world, we want it to be for the benefit of everyone and the benefit of humanity. It's something bigger than all of us and the competition, remembering we've got to take these final steps in a careful, thoughtful way. We must make sure that we're building it with safety and security in mind, as well as enabling all of the amazing, good use cases that we hope to see. Demis, picking up on your word "opportunity," the 5,000 students and professional developers here on campus are living proof of that. Yet, a lot of America doesn't see it or feel it. Look at the front page of today's Wall Street Journal: "The AI backlash building, right? American rebellion." Eric Schmidt getting booed. Why is that happening, and what can you do about it? Well, I think a lot of the ways that some of my peers are talking about this, I don't really agree with. I don't really agree with the style of communication, but also some of the substance, too. What I would say, the way I'm looking at it, is there's going to be, no doubt, a huge amount of change. Something this transformative—I've been on record saying if you want to try and quantify it, it's going to be ten times the impact of the Industrial Revolution, maybe ten times faster. So, over a decade instead of a century, and that might be an underestimate—it probably is. Wait, ten times the impact and ten times faster? Yeah, so you can think of it as 100x the Industrial Revolution, maybe an underestimate. So, that's huge. But what I say about that is the future's not written. It's very uncertain. Are there more jobs going to be created? Are there going to be different ones? We don't know; nobody knows. None of these so-called experts know. The future is to be written, actually, by the next generation. I'm actually very excited when I talk to students and when I go to places. We just came back earlier this year from the India AI Summit, and the youth of India are so excited about what they can do, what these tools will enable them to do—build companies, build applications, and compete at a global scale. This technology is actually democratizing opportunities for what you can build. What would have taken a team of 10 to 50 experts to build, maybe a smart kid in their garage can build themselves. When I think back to the era I grew up in with home computers, and then there was the internet, I think of the amount of opportunities that created for the kids and students of the day who grew up native with that technology to actually go on and build the future we see today. I think that will happen again with the next generation that is going to grow up AI-native. I think for the next ten years at least—and it's hard to see beyond that—they're going to be superpowered. As long as they're technically trained enough, and they bring creativity, taste, and judgment—these kinds of skills—to the table, I think that's going to be the next era. So, I'm actually very optimistic about human ingenuity to find amazing ways to use these new tools, and we're seeing that all the time. Your hunch would be net more jobs, or you can't even say that? I think there will be changes to the job market, as there always are with any new revolution. But I don't see why there wouldn't be more opportunities. Maybe it will be more like creating startups—one or two-person startups. More entrepreneurial skills will be needed because you have these incredible creative tools. There are going to be changes, but I think, overall, it will be better. The other thing I would say on the backlash is that I think it's incumbent on our field—the AI field and the industry—to show the unequivocal benefits more clearly and more concretely. That's what we try to do with our AI for Science work, applying AI to advancing science and medicine. That's why I've spent my whole career working on using these AI tools for things like AlphaFold, as you mentioned, and drug discovery. I think there are so many areas of science that AI can be applied to, and we're trying to do that: material science, mathematics, energy sources. That's what I think we need to show more of, and I hope our other peer organizations do more of as well. Now, Demis, taking your point about the style of communication and your fellow "Greek gods" on the cover of The Economist, you're proudly a scientist. You're not a doomer, and you're not an accelerationist. Why do some AI leaders talk about the negatives of AI? How does that help them? Well, I'm in the middle. I call myself a cautious optimist. Cautious optimist. Yes, I'm optimistic—obviously I'm optimistic, otherwise I wouldn't have been doing this for thirty years—that the benefits are going to far outweigh the negatives with something like AI, as long as we take our time and give ourselves the time as a society to get these next few years right. We're talking about the next 100, maybe 1,000 years of human flourishing if we get this right. We've got a lot of challenges to get that right, so we've got to give ourselves the time to do it, rather than just sort of racing to get the next thing out. I think that's where we've got to put our attention and come together to discuss the bigger impacts that all of this work is going to produce. We have a treat: a clip from "The Thinking Game," a remarkable documentary about Demis and DeepMind filmed over five years. We have a fascinating scene from your old offices in London. This was not rehearsed or recreated; it was totally serendipitous. The cameras just happened to be there. Let's watch. "Now we had a tool that can be used practically by scientists. There are these people asking us, 'You know, I've got this protein involved in malaria or, you know, some infectious disease. We don't know the structure. Can we use AlphaFold to solve it?'" "We can easily predict all known sequences in a month." "All known sequences in a month?" "Yeah, easily." "A billion? Two billion?" "And then..." "Why don't we just do that, then?" "Yeah, we should just do that." "Well, we, I mean, we've like—now you can really..." "That's way better. Why don't we just do that?" "Well, so that's one of the options..." "Right, you know, and there's this..." "We should just—we should, that's a great idea. We should just run every protein in existence and then release that. Why has no one suggested this before? Of course that's what we should do. Why are we thinking about making a service and then people submit their protein? We'll just fold everything and then give it to everyone in the world. Who knows how many discoveries will be made from that?" So, a quote for the ages and an iconic moment. We saw the DeepMind working, Demis, when you said, "So why don't we do that? So why don't we just do that?" Tell us what was in your mind and what was happening. Look, this is that particular example—and actually, I'll just get back to your last question as well about acceleration—this is the type of acceleration that we want, right, in my opinion. You couldn't get any more acceleration, in a sense, than what I spent my whole career doing. I think there are so many challenges facing society today, from disease to energy to our environmental ecosystems and climate change. We need technology like AI to come and help us solve these other problems. And the number one, in my opinion, as I said yesterday, is applying AI to improving human health. With AlphaFold, predicting protein structures is one of the critical pieces of developing drugs to help with these diseases—only one piece, but a critical piece. What was happening there in that meeting is I just sort of worked out that because AlphaFold was so accurate, and also so fast, it could fold a protein in a matter of seconds. I knew there were a couple hundred million proteins known to science, and just from a back-of-the-envelope calculation, I could feel that if we put a certain amount of computers on it, we could fold all of them in a year. So, there was no need to set up the normal way of doing this, which is to have a server where people submit their genetic sequences—the amino acid sequences of the protein they care about—and then you go away, fold it for a week, and give them back the structure. We could just put all of it out there for free into the world on a huge database, run by the European Bioinformatics Institute in Cambridge, and let all the researchers around the world access whatever protein they wanted at almost the speed of a Google search. That felt obvious to me as the biggest impact we could make with this breakthrough. I think that's an example of great acceleration. But on the other hand, I'm not in the Pollyanna camp that says there's no risk here, let's let it rip, there's no danger. Of course, when we're bringing in something this momentous as AGI—a new era we're going into—we have to think that through very carefully. There are going to be challenges and risks, and there will be ways we can try and mitigate them, but again, only if we give ourselves enough time collectively to do that. The fact that cameras followed you for five years and that you were willing to let them in shows you recognize that you're on the frontier of something that will shape humanity. It's hard to see ourselves as others see us. What did you learn from the documentary, either from the process or actually watching it? Well, I actually hate seeing myself on camera, so it was a hard thing to agree to. But it's the same film crew that did the AlphaGo movie, if some of you have seen that, for the AlphaGo match. They came along right at the last minute to do that, and I thought it was a really moving documentary recording of quite an important moment in modern AI history: the big match we had against Lee Se-dol. We actually just celebrated the ten-year anniversary of that, which is kind of unbelievable really—in some ways it feels like a century has gone by, and in other ways it feels like yesterday. But they proposed to basically be a fly on the wall, and we trusted them with that. They happened to be around at key moments; that meeting was kind of amazing because they only came over every now and again to record a few things. One of the reasons we agreed to do that is we felt that this technology is a societal-level transformation. We felt that people would want to know something about the people behind it, what their motivations were, why they were doing this, and what good we think can come from it. I hope that documentary shows that. It seems to have gone down well, and I think it does a good job of portraying what our motivations and passions are, like AI for Science. Demis, a quick tour of your mind and world. Let's talk about simulations and robotics. What's going on there, and why are simulations such a key milestone? I think simulations are really important. I've been sort of enamored with simulations as long as I have been with AI. In fact, the first real professional thing I programmed was Theme Park, a game I wrote in the '90s. It had AI in it and it was a simulation game—one of the first for both—and it came out in 1994. So, I've always seen them as quite complementary. Both in the sense that you can use AI to learn simulations about complex phenomena—for example, our weather prediction system that we showcased yesterday that helps predict the paths of hurricanes and other extreme weather events much more quickly and accurately than traditional systems—but also, it can provide data for AI systems. We've been using Genie, our interactive 3D model, to test Waymo cars in very unusual, one-in-a-billion type situations that you would never see in the real world. For example, what if there's a forest fire around the road you're driving down, or a biplane has done an emergency landing on the motorway, or an elephant has appeared in the road? You're never going to see these kinds of things in your everyday training data, but you want to know how your system is going to react. These types of simulations are very useful for testing that. If your system doesn't do well there, you can use them to generate more data that the system can learn from. Okay, this is going to be your favorite question—I know you love to talk about the scientific method. Let's talk about AI for science and health. You've talked about human health as the ultimate use case for AI. Yes, I think that AI is the ultimate tool for science in the limit. That is why I started this whole journey: to try and, as a scientist, understand better the world around us. I felt that our minds could use an incredible tool to help even the smartest experts more quickly navigate all the complexity of the data we collect, and try to find connections, insights, and structure in that data. What better tool than AI to do that? I think that's coming to fruition now, and it's just a very, very exciting time to see the pace of science pick up in all sorts of fields—especially in many fields where we've been stuck for 30 or 40 years, like we were with protein folding. I think we're going to see a lot of those breakthroughs over the next decade or so. When we look at AI in the real world, what are some of the ways that people are already feeling it? Is part of the AI backlash that they don't even realize they are using it? Well, we're using AI all the time in pretty much every product we use. From our point of view, everything from Maps to Gmail has now been enhanced and is in the middle of being upgraded with AI. What I hope to see with the next generation of assistants—you saw us talk about Gemini Live yesterday, but also the smart glasses—is that eventually this will give us back time. That's what I hope. I hope that the smarter our AI assistants are, the less admin we need to do. We will spend less time with a lot of the technologies that are kind of mundane, giving us time back to focus on what we think is important, whether that's creativity, our families, or just connecting with other humans. That's what I hope will happen in the next few years. We can still stay connected and get the information that we need to do our jobs or that interests us, but more conveniently and at the time we want because we'll have an AI assistant helping us manage all of that. If you look at all the keynote announcements yesterday here at Google I/O, the keynote put a bow around a lot of it, but it was also a lot—excuse me, a lot to take in. Ina Fried at Axios had a great headline about "AI everywhere." If we hit pause, what is something from these announcements that we should remember and focus on? I think the main thing is that the agentic era is really here. What we were really excited about at this I/O is how we're integrating that across the Google ecosystem. With Workspace and all of the things that people use every day and love, how is that going to interact securely with an agentic system? I think we've seen the potential of agents, but we're just scratching the surface of what they can do, from coding all the way to managing your admin load. I'm very excited about all of those things. And then there is obviously the potential of these world models to help in the real world with things like robotics. Hopefully, we'll be talking about some big advances in areas like that next year. And responsibility—how do you think about that in your work? Does it inhibit you, does it accelerate you, or how do you view it? I think it's a critical part of everything we do; it's not really about inhibiting or accelerating. When you're working on this kind of technology—as we have been for a long time—you have to think about the implications. We obviously look at the enormous, amazing benefits we're hoping for, but we also recognize it is a dual-purpose technology. Those same tools can also be repurposed by bad actors for harmful ends. Those two things weigh heavily on us as we work at the forefront and the frontier, trying to understand both the negative and the positive consequences of what this technology can do. As the last stop on our tour, let's look at future vision. Tell us what you know is behind the curtain and what we should be watching for in the coming months. Well, the big thing everyone is working on at the moment is this idea of self-improvement. We're seeing a slow version of that already with coding agents helping our own engineers get better and more productive with coding. The question is: what is the next level of that? I think coding and mathematics are quite special cases because the outputs are verifiable. You can verify whether they're correct, and you can also generate as much synthetic data as you want. There is evidence that this creates a kind of compounding flywheel, so I think that's a very interesting space that all the leading labs are investing heavily in. I think the other area is just to build the reliability, robustness, and consistency of how these systems work, focusing on tool use and instruction-following, and making sure they're reliable over long-term plans. I think that is going to be the next step change for these things. Part of that is making sure they are robust, safe, and secure. The more powerful these agents get and the more we delegate tasks to them, the more we have to make sure from a cybersecurity standpoint that they are doing exactly what we expect them to do, that we can monitor them, and that tasks are being done securely. Okay, so that's a good way to think about step changes. You pointed to consistency, right? What would our step change buckets be for, say, 2026? Well, as we get closer to something like AGI, we shouldn't be seeing systems that are brilliant at certain things when posed in certain ways, but are still tripped up if you present them with a simpler problem—say in mathematics—but present it in a way that is a little bit out of distribution. I think that shouldn't be the case for an AGI system, a general intelligence. That's not the case with human intelligence. If you're good at a certain game or a certain kind of task, and someone gives you a strictly simpler problem in that same domain, you'll be able to solve that problem, too. There won't be this kind of jagged intelligence. Another big area that's missing—and one we are focusing on—is continuous learning. How do you have systems that learn after their training, after they've been released out into the world? How can they adapt and learn further? That's also related to personalization, making sure that the systems are personalized to the extent you want as the user, relative to your particular context. Last one on the future. A year from now, at Google I/O 2025, what do you think we'll be talking about? What will be the centerpiece? I think there will be a lot more agents in the real world embedded in our workflows. We're starting to see that now, and I think over the next year that will mature, so that will be a huge thing. I also hope there will be big advances in AI in the real world. I'm hoping we'll see some big leaps forward in applying these models that understand the world, like Gemini Robotics—which is the state-of-the-art robotics model out there right now—applied to many different form factors in robotics. And talking about AI in the real world, I think something you've mentioned as an example is Hurricane Beryl? Mhm. And how AI can be used in weather forecasting. Yeah, that was one of the examples we showed yesterday of an incredible way that we can use AI in simulation. Weather is a very complicated dynamic system that traditionally involves solving a lot of fluid dynamics equations, like Navier-Stokes. These kinds of traditional systems take weeks to run, and they are limited in how far ahead they can look. For a situation like an extreme weather event—like a hurricane—you want to know ahead of time. As we showed in the video yesterday, even a day ahead of time is very important for emergency services to reduce the damage to human lives and property that these extreme weather events bring. I think that's just one example of the big strides we're making by applying AI to learn simulations from available data, rather than directly programming the mechanics of a simulation by hand. What would be your advice to your younger self? In an interview you gave in connection with being awarded the Lasker Award, you said, "Believe in your ideas, even though they can seem quite outlandish." You said you personally had done that. How has AI made that even more true? You said having confidence in your ideas from the very earliest stage makes all the difference. Well, I think you need to have confidence in your ideas on anything big if you're going to stand a chance of achieving it. That's very clear. But I also think it's not just about confidence; it's also about your passion and your belief in how important that work is. For example, we're here today where AI is the biggest thing the world is talking about, and we always imagined that could happen when we started DeepMind in 2010 and even before then. But I would still be working on AI now, whether or not that had happened—even if it were still just a niche academic subject—because I think it is the most fascinating and important problem to be working on. It has worked out and the timing was right. We thought back then that it was a kind of 20-year mission, and we're sort of exactly on schedule, but I would have been doing it anyway. It's a little bit beyond confidence; it's a sense of almost obsession with the problem, of it being the right problem and the most interesting problem, at least for my mind and the passions I have. My expression of trying to advance science was to build this amazing tool that was also an interesting scientific artifact in of itself. What more interesting thing could one spend their life and career on? That's how I felt thirty years ago when I first started on all of this. And so, Demis, how you chose that road is something historians will study. Sebastian Mallaby is out with a great book, "The Power Law," and he said that you spent over 30 hours with him at a London pub. I will say that you might be the first biographical subject in history to spend 30 hours and not regret it! This book boils down your choice to this: you chose to use your mind and your skills to understand the universe. Tell us about that road taken, and the roads you didn't take. Yeah, I should say we weren't drinking in the pub; it was just coffee! It went fast because we actually got on really well. I wasn't counting the hours, but he suggested that, and we had a very fun, wide-ranging discussion on all sorts of topics. For me, I started off on this journey because I've always been fascinated by the big questions in science: the nature of reality, and the nature of consciousness. I've always been almost puzzled, actually, that as we go through life, most of us don't really stop to ponder those big questions, even though they're staring us in the face. For example, what is time? We don't know what time is. We have some kind of hand-wavy idea that it's a direction of entropy and goes one way, but we don't really know what it is, which is kind of astounding to me. Let alone gravity, quantum mechanics, and all of these mysteries. I've always been fascinated by that since I can remember, and normally, when you're fascinated by these questions, you go into physics. Physics was my favorite subject at school, but I started reading biographies of all the greats—my favorites being people like Feynman, Einstein, and Weinberg, all of these brilliant physicists. What I realized is that, at least for me, post-war and post-1970s, we hadn't really made much progress on those fronts. We made a lot of progress at the beginning of the 1900s with Einstein, Heisenberg, and so on, but towards the latter part of the 20th century, we hadn't made that much progress. I was wondering why this was, and I felt that maybe we are reaching the limit of what the human mind can comprehend and understand—even the most brilliant minds like Feynman—without the aid of some new tools. That was my real feeling and my takeaway from reading those biographies. Combining that with reading a lot of science fiction—like Asimov's Foundation series and Iain Banks's Culture series—and philosophy books like Gödel, Escher, Bach, which was very formative for me, it all kind of came together. I thought we could try to directly answer some of these questions about the nature of intelligence and consciousness by building AI and comparing it to the human brain. It would also be a tool itself that could be used for science to advance the frontiers of what we know. So, it was sort of obvious to me thirty plus years ago that that would be the most interesting and perhaps most valuable path forward. Inspired by the point you made about biographies, who is a scientist living and working today who doesn't work for Google, who either should get more attention, or that you admire, or who future biographies will be written about? I mean, there are so many great scientists out there. I don't know if you're asking me who is sort of underrated, but I do feel like a lot of the big biology scientists—who are well-known and have won Nobel Prizes, like Paul Nurse and Jennifer Doudna—are doing amazing work inventing technologies like CRISPR. I can't wait to see what the next generation is going to do with AI tools to make their own way and make their own big breakthroughs with the aid of these amazing tools that we have. Demis, we're going to finish on a useful note for the builders and future builders in this room and those watching around the world, who will watch this conversation in perpetuity. You spend most of your time in London, but when you come to the Bay Area, you feel a palpable builder energy, which is exciting for all of us when we visit. A lot of people here are living it and creating that, but you also have a warning for us: you detect a certain freneticism. Yeah, it's an amazing energy. I spend about half my time out here and half in London, and it's always great to plug into the energy of the builder and entrepreneur ecosystem here. Obviously, it's second to none, and it's incredible to see that enthusiasm and embracing of opportunities. But I would also say that I do detect, as you say, this kind of frenetic rush as well. I think that is not conducive to thinking deeply about hard topics for a long amount of time. I think a lot of the next advances are going to be in deep tech using AI, perhaps applied to another area of hard science. There are a lot of opportunities there, and you're going to have to go at that for many, many years before there is a result. So, I think having some quieter, reflective moments where you can think very deeply about what you're planning—rather than just rushing into the next thing you see—is crucial. I think a lot of the time, speed is favored over direction. Making sure you have the direction right is often more important than just the speed. Speed and velocity are important, but if you're running 100 miles an hour in the wrong direction, that's actually worse than standing still and taking a moment to think about getting the direction right. I often think there's a bit of an obsession out here with just velocity. While it is critical, more critical is the direction of that velocity vector. Very useful, thank you. At Axios, we always finish with one fun thing, so I wanted to ask you about longevity escape velocity. The idea is that if you and I can live five more years, say, we'll live forever. How much stock do you put in that notion, and what are you doing to make sure that you make it? I don't really worry about that kind of question; I'm very pragmatic when it comes to these things. What I'm trying to do with Isomorphic, and what we try to do with AlphaFold, is use these technologies to cure terrible diseases. That's the number one thing I can think of using AI for. When it comes to aging, I know lots of aging researchers and scientists, and there are lots of companies trying to work on this. There are lots of theories out there: is it the culmination of all diseases, is it a disease itself, or does it need to be reprogrammed? I think that we should start just by solving the existing terrible diseases we know about. That will help us live longer and healthier lives, and then let's see what's remaining and take it from there. And what is your personal health obsession? I see you sometimes wear a WHOOP, right? Yeah, sometimes, yes. And what else? Well, you know, I try to stay as healthy as possible in the very few spare minutes I have. I don't sleep enough; that's one of the main issues I have to try and address. What's your average sleep? I try to get six hours, but sometimes I fail. Thank you to Google I/O and Demis Hassabis, and thanks for an incredible conversation. Thanks, Mike. Thank you.

Andrej Karpathy Joins Anthropic | SpaceX Files S1: How Does it Trade | Cerebras Smashes Day 1

20VC with Harry Stebbings(204.0K)·87:17·5.3K views·2026-05-21
Harry StebbingsHost, 20VC Jason LemkinSaaS Investor Andrej KarpathyAI Researcher
TL;DRAs elite AI firms like Anthropic and OpenAI command staggering private valuations and push compute grants directly to startups, legacy SaaS companies face a structural squeeze, compounded by a looming public and political backlash over AI-driven white-collar layoffs.

TL;DR

As elite AI firms like Anthropic and OpenAI command staggering private valuations and push compute grants directly to startups, legacy SaaS companies face a structural squeeze, compounded by a looming public and political backlash over AI-driven white-collar layoffs.

The AI Giants: Valuation Wars and Token Ecosystems

  • Anthropic’s $900 Billion Valuation Leap: Anthropic is in talks to raise $30 billion at a $900 billion valuation (up from $380 billion in February), backed by investors like Green Oaks, Sequoia, Alimter, and Dragoneer.
      • Low-Risk Trade: Investors view this as a highly secure "post-IPO" trade because the business model is entirely derisked from existential and IPO failure; the only remaining variable is valuation risk.
      • Dario Amodei vs. Sam Altman: Anthropic's CEO, Dario Amodei, favors clean, low-drama, straight-cash transactions completed in days. In contrast, OpenAI's Sam Altman pushes complex financial engineering (e.g., mixing upfront cash, contingent venture debt, and SoftBank loans) to maximize valuations.
  • The Reality of Enterprise Token Spend: Salesforce spent $300 million on Anthropic tokens this year, primarily for engineering and code generation.
      • Cost Per Head: This translates to roughly $15,000 to $20,000 per year per developer—representing only 4% of an engineer's fully burdened $500,000 salary.
      • The TAM Conundrum: For OpenAI and Anthropic to hit their projected $1 trillion token revenue targets, they must capture 20% of global corporate R&D/engineering payrolls. If enterprises do not replace or supplement a fifth of their engineering salary budgets with tokens, current foundation model valuations will face severe corrections.
  • OpenAI’s Y Combinator Offensive: OpenAI is offering $2 million in tokens to every startup in the current Y Combinator batch in exchange for equity.
      • Valuation Inflation: This massive capital injection is expected to drive up seed round valuations (potentially anchoring them at $60 million to $100 million post-demo day) and dramatically shrink the ownership share available to traditional early-stage VCs.
      • Capacity Signal: This move suggests OpenAI currently holds surplus compute capacity, whereas Anthropic remains heavily capacity-constrained.

Public SaaS Bifurcation: Winners vs. "Terminal" Legacy Players

  • The Rebound of Software Giants: Top-tier SaaS companies are demonstrating strong acceleration, proving that a broader software creation boom is underway.
      • Data Dog: Reported its first $1 billion revenue quarter (growing 32% year-over-year) and crossed $4 billion in ARR.
      • Figma: Accelerated for its second consecutive quarter, reaching a two-year high Net Dollar Retention (NDR) of 139%.
      • Figma's Missed Pipeline: Despite its strong quarter, Figma missed a massive $500 million opportunity by failing to natively build a design-to-production pipeline (leaving space for startups like Replit and Lovable).
  • The Terminal Decline of Wix and Squarespace: Wix has plummeted 45% since its recent stock buyback, dropping to a $2.2 billion market cap and trading at roughly 1x revenue.
      • The Double-Squeeze: Legacy website builders are being decimated by "vibe coding" tools on the low end (allowing non-technical users to generate sites via prompts) and Shopify on the high end, which has successfully captured the merchant and e-commerce markets.
  • The Futility of Defensive Stock Buybacks: Heavy criticism was aimed at companies like Wix and Salesforce for executing massive stock buybacks to appease activist investors. Preserving cash balance sheets is deemed far more valuable for strategic AI acquisitions in a fast-evolving tech environment.

Compute Starvation and the Infrastructure Supercycle

  • Hardware and Optical Connects on Fire: While traditional legacy software faces downward valuation pressure, the physical hardware sector—semiconductors, optical connections, and power infrastructure (e.g., SanDisk, Marvell, Micron, Broadcom, Cisco)—is experiencing a historic boom.
  • Nebius and the Capex Bottleneck: Nebius is accelerating at 684% growth due to acute compute starvation.
      • Bureaucratic Salvation: The slow permitting and construction of modern data centers may protect the industry from overcapacity. If data center rollouts are delayed while corporate token spend remains stable, compute will remain scarce, keeping margins high for providers like Nebius and CoreWeave.
  • The VC Capex Trap: Building competitive infrastructure startups now requires $500 million to $1 billion in upfront capex. Consequently, seed-stage investors are increasingly avoiding early-stage infrastructure deals due to the extreme capital intensity required to survive.

The $1.75 Trillion SpaceX IPO

  • The S1 Financial Lag: SpaceX has targeted June 12th for a historic IPO, aiming for a $1.75 trillion valuation and a $75 billion raise, with 30% of the float allocated to retail investors.
      • Outdated Financials: The initial S1 prospectus will only reflect December financials ($15B–$18B revenue, 20%–30% growth for Starlink/SpaceX standalone). It will fail to reflect major 2024 events, including the consolidation of X.AI, the Cursor acquisition, and the Anthropic deal.
  • Speculative "Casino" Pricing: Retail enthusiasm and Robinhood-style "meme" trading could decouple the stock from traditional discounted cash flow (DCF) metrics. The panel predicts the valuation could quickly spike to $3 trillion or $5 trillion based purely on retail demand before experiencing a sharp correction.

The Looming Political and Social Backlash Against Tech

  • Euphemistic Layoffs and "Political Morons": Tech leaders are implementing aggressive workforce reductions (Meta laying off 8,000; Cisco cutting 4,000; Intuit shedding 1,600) while publicly attributing these cuts to AI efficiencies.
      • Standard Chartered: The bank’s CEO notoriously framed eliminating 7,800 jobs not as losses, but as "job reductions in favor of the machines."
  • The PR Failure of AI Founders: Unlike previous tech waves (like Meta/Facebook pretending to "bring the world together"), AI founders have spent three years hyping AGI's ability to destroy jobs and humanity, leading to immediate public hostility and events like Eric Schmidt being booed at a college graduation.
  • The "Social Charter" Mandate: To avoid punitive wealth taxes and severe political backlashes, major tech companies may soon face a "social obligation" to artificially reinflate their headcounts and employ thousands of workers, even if AI agents can technically perform those roles.

Notable Quotes

"Once you lose that veneer, you are going to be valued for the rest of your life on some variety of revenue growth and cash flow... It’s almost impossible to ever get back to 50 times ARR again." — Rory
"We have people who are brilliant scientists who politically are utter morons. And the people who are utter morons at AI but brilliant at politics are going to have us for lunch. That’s the movie of the next three years." — Rory
"At least when Meta was busy destroying the world, they were smart enough to pretend it was all about bringing friends together and not destroying democracy. We will regret that lack of transparency." — Rory
"Politics when you've been laid off by email at 4:00 AM because the CEO of your company has decided he'd prefer to buy $100 million of machines than $100 million of people... that politics becomes very different." — Jason Lemkin

If ARR multiples are the proxy for value, then this is the best value in the venture universe. Which is why I'm going to say very smart capital allocators whose mandate isn't kind of sector-specific, but kind of range anywhere, stick your money in. You know, people like Greenoaks, people like Altimeter are doing this deal because they're like, "You know, yesterday I can do a 20 million ARR deal, today I can do a $50 billion ARR deal and the multiple is better when it's so obviously a good deal," as we mentioned there, given the trade for investors. Why would Dario and Anthropic do it at that price if it is so obviously a good deal? Because you're giving away 30 over 900, which is like—let me see—3% of your business to derisk it for another year of monstrous burn where, you know, you're committing to, I don't know, six 5-gigawatt data centers this year. And you know, the mental rule of thumb is the total cost of a gigawatt of high-end compute is 40 or 50 billion. So to me, it's a no-brainer to do it. They're going to raise, and they're going to raise again, and then they're going to raise again. Do you think they'll raise again before they go public? I doubt it, just depending on the trajectory now because they're saying they're going public in November. But my point is, even after—by the way, the other comment is when you stop having to raise, that's a disaster because it means that you're—why are you raising? You're raising for capex and you're raising for growth. Once that hypergrowth stops and you don't have a capex need, you also don't have a growth story, and that'll be a very different place to be. I mean, right now, yeah, right now this is the highest ROI on equity dollars, so that's why they're getting it.
